(1) Among the subtleties is what is narrated in al-Mabsut and others: that a man married his two sons to the two daughters of a man, and the women entered each brother to the other brother, and each had intercourse with the one conducted to him, thinking she was his wife. So he sought a fatwa from the scholars, and that era was the era of Imam Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. They answered that each one must avoid the woman with whom he had intercourse, and she would observe 'iddah to return to her husband. Abu Hanifa answered: if each one is pleased with the woman with whom he had intercourse, then each divorces his wife and marries the woman with whom he had intercourse, and enters her immediately, because he is the one who made the contract and there is no 'iddah for the divorce for them, because each of the two women was not had intercourse by the one who divorced her. Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rulings of 'iddah for women who have intercourse under shubha (mistaken belief) or in a fasid (defective) marriage. The author first lists several scenarios: a woman is brought to a man other than her husband unknowingly; a man finds a woman in his bed thinking she is his wife; a man has intercourse with his divorced wife during her 'iddah out of shubha; a man buys a slave-girl and later finds out she was free. In all these cases, the woman must observe 'iddah of three menstrual cycles. If she is married to someone else, that husband cannot approach her during this 'iddah, but his marriage remains valid. Then the author relates a famous story from al-Mabsut: a man married his two sons to another man's two daughters, but the brides were mistakenly given to the wrong brothers. Each brother had intercourse with the woman he thought was his wife. The scholars of the time (including Imam Abu Hanifa) were asked. The majority said each must avoid the woman and she must observe 'iddah to return to her original husband. Abu Hanifa gave a brilliant answer: if each brother is pleased with the woman he had intercourse with, he can divorce his current wife and marry that woman immediately, because there is no 'iddah for a woman who was not actually touched by her divorcer. This shows Abu Hanifa's insight. The passage then returns to the matn, discussing 'iddah in cases of death of the husband or separation (either by court order or abandonment). For a woman who does not menstruate (due to youth, old age, or having reached puberty by age without menstruation), her 'iddah is three months instead. The commentator clarifies that some scholars hold no distinction between batil (invalid) and fasid marriage regarding obligation of 'iddah, and lists examples of fasid marriages. The text also notes that if a woman does not menstruate due to a temporary cause like breastfeeding or illness, she remains in 'iddah until she menstruates, according to Imam Muhammad in the Muwatta' following Ibn Mas'ud's opinion.

Key Terms
عدة (iddah): A waiting period after divorce or death; not required if the marriage was not consummated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's fatwa on the mistaken brides demonstrates that 'iddah is not required if the divorcer never consummated the marriage, allowing for an immediate resolution.

Iddah for Non-Menstruating Free Women and Menstruating Slave Women
Ruling on iddah for non-menstruating free women and menstruating slave women with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the iddah durations for free non-menstruating women and menstruating slave women for divorce and death, and identify the Quranic and hadith proofs for each.

Core Idea
A free woman without menses waits three months for divorce, a widow four months ten days; a slave woman waits two menses.

Iddah is an obligatory waiting period that serves several purposes, including ensuring the womb is clear. The duration depends on the woman's status (free or slave) and whether she menstruates. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a commentary on Hidayah, clarifies the exact periods and their scriptural bases, addressing edge cases like a woman whose menses temporarily stops.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories
The passage distinguishes between free women who do not menstruate (due to youth, menopause, or prolonged purity) and slave women who do menstruate. For each, it gives the iddah for divorce/annulment and for death.
Free non-menstruating: 3 months (divorce) / 4 months 10 days (death). Slave menstruating: 2 menses (divorce/annulment).
2
Examine the conditions
The commentary notes that the iddah by months applies only when the woman has reached the age of menstruation but does not menstruate permanently or for a prolonged period. Temporary cessation (e.g., due to breastfeeding) does not shift to months until it is clear she will not menstruate.
Also, iddah is only obligatory after consummation; the passage assumes the woman is a wife (not mere concubinage for slaves).
3
Trace the Quranic proofs
For the free non-menstruating woman, the proof is Surah al-Talaq 65:4, which explicitly states three months. For the widow, Surah al-Baqarah 2:234 gives four months and ten days.
These verses are qat'i in transmission and meaning, establishing the obligation firmly.
4
Trace the hadith proof for slave women
For the menstruating slave woman, the proof is a hadith (riwayah ahad): 'The iddah of a slave woman is two menses.' This is narrated by multiple sources, including Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi.
The hadith's transmission is zanni but its meaning is qat'i in indicating two menses.
5
Note the ikhtilaf and practical application
There is an intra-Hanafi difference regarding the iddah of a woman with temporary cessation, and an inter-madhhab difference with the Shafi'i school. The mufta bihi position is as stated in the matn.
Umar's statement about one and a half menses is noted but not adopted because a half cannot be counted.

And (for) who does not menstruate: three months, and for death: four months and ten days, and for a slave woman who menstruates: two menses. 'And who does not menstruate three months': i.e., the iddah for a free woman who does not menstruate due to youth or similar for divorce and annulment is three months. 'And for death four months and ten days': his saying 'and for death' is conjuncted to his saying 'for divorce', and the meaning of annulment is the iddah for a free woman for death is four months and ten days. 'And for a slave woman who menstruates: two menses.' [1] His saying 'and who does not menstruate' is attached to his saying 'who reached the age', and this includes one who never saw blood or saw it and it stopped before completing the duration of menstruation, for if she saw blood one day then it stopped until a year passed then he divorced her, her iddah is by months. Thus in al-Tatarkhaniyyah, and by this restriction is excluded a young woman with extended purity, for her iddah is not by months as we mentioned previously. (2) His saying 'three months' because of His saying in Surah al-Talaq: 'And those who have despaired of menstruation among your women, if you doubt, their iddah is three months, and those who have not menstruated.' (3) His saying 'four months' because of His saying in Surah al-Baqarah: 'And those who are taken in death among you and leave wives, they shall wait by themselves four months and ten days.' [4] His saying 'and for a slave woman' i.e., his wife is a slave woman, whether she is a qinn, mudabbara, mukataba, or umm walad of her master who married her, or mustas'ah according to Imam, and here also the condition of consummation is necessary as passed that there is no iddah on a wife not consummated in reality or legally, and it is restricted by wife; for there is no iddah on a slave woman by intercourse with right hand possession except if she is umm walad, as passed. [0] His saying 'two menses' because of the hadith: 'The iddah of a slave woman is two menses,' narrated by Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah, al-Hakim and others, and Umar said: 'If I could, I would make the iddah of a slave woman one and a half menses,' narrated by Abd al-Razzaq, Ibn Abi Shaybah, al-Bayhaqi and others, and he pointed thereby that completing the second menstruation is due to impossibility of fragmentation.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the rulings regarding the iddah for free women who do not menstruate and for slave women who menstruate. The author begins by stating the ruling: a free woman who does not menstruate due to youth (before puberty) or old age (menopause) or having reached the age of puberty but not yet menstruated, her iddah for divorce or annulment (faskh) is three months, and for the death of her husband it is four months and ten days. A slave woman who menstruates observes an iddah of two menses for divorce or annulment. The commentary then clarifies the conditions: the iddah by months applies only to free women who are not menstruating, and the condition of consummation (dakhul) is required for iddah to be obligatory, as stated earlier. For a slave woman, the iddah of two menses is specific to the case where she is a wife (i.e., married), not for mere concubinage. The author provides Quranic evidence: Surah al-Talaq (65:4) for the three months of the woman who has despaired of menstruation or not yet menstruated, and Surah al-Baqarah (2:234) for the widow's iddah of four months and ten days. For the slave woman, a hadith from Abu Dawud et al. is cited: 'The iddah of a slave woman is two menses.' The passage also notes a statement from Umar that he would have preferred one and a half menses, but since a half is impossible, two full menses are required.

Key Terms
عدة (iddah): The waiting period observed by a woman after divorce or death of husband to ascertain her womb is free from pregnancy.
حرة (hurrah): Free woman (as opposed to slave).
أمة (amah): Slave woman; here specifically a married slave woman.
حيضة (haydah): A menstrual period; a single occurrence of menstruation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Three months
B) Three menstrual cycles
C) Four months and ten days
D) Two menses

Key Takeaway
The iddah durations for free non-menstruating women and menstruating slave women are explicitly based on Quran and hadith, with the condition that the non-menstruation must be permanent for the months-based iddah to apply.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Iddah Periods for Free Women and Slave Women
Establishes 'iddah periods for free and slave women based on Quran and Hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the 'iddah periods for free and slave women in divorce and death, identify the Quranic and Hadith proofs, and apply the half-rule for slave women.

Core Idea
The iddah of a free woman is three months or four months ten days; a slave woman's iddah is half, except for pregnant women whose iddah ends with delivery.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya explains the waiting periods ('iddah) for women after divorce or death. The rulings are derived directly from the Quran and Sunnah, with a clear distinction between free and slave women. Understanding these periods is essential for marriage contracts and family law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is 'iddah? What causes it? Identify the hukm level (wajib).
The 'iddah is an obligatory waiting period after divorce or death.
2
Locate the dalil
Which Quranic verses and hadith establish the periods for free women and slave women?
Surah al-Talaq 65:4, Surah al-Baqarah 2:234, and the hadith 'Iddat al-amah haydatan'.
3
Distinguish free vs slave
How does slavery halve the 'iddah? Apply the principle 'al-riqq munassif lil-ahkam'.
For slave women, the period is exactly half of the free woman's period.
4
Apply to non-menstruating and pregnant women
When do months replace menstruation? When does delivery end the 'iddah?
Women who do not menstruate (young, menopausal) observe months; pregnant women observe until delivery.
5
Note the ikhtilaf
How do other schools differ on a slave woman's 'iddah?
Some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) hold two months for a non-menstruating slave woman, while Hanafi holds half of free woman's months.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب العدة
ولم تحض ثلاثة أشهر ، وللموت أربعة أشهر وعشر، ولأمة تحيض حيضتان. ولم تحض ثلاثةَ أَشْهُر ) : أي العدَّة لحرَّة لا تحيض لصغر ونحوه للطلاق والفسخ ثلاثة أشهر. (وللموت أربعة أشهر وعشر) : قولُهُ: وللموت عطف على قوله : للطلاق، والفسخ معناه العدة للحرة للموتِ أربعة أشهر وعشر.
(ولأمة تحيض حيضتان).
[١] قوله : ولم تحض ؛ متعلق بقوله : «بلغت بالسن»، وهذا شامل لما لم تردماً أصلاً أو رأته وانقطع قبل تمام مدة الحيض، فإنّه لو رأت يوماً دماً ثم انقطع حتى مضت سنة ثم طلقها ، فعدتها بالأشهر. كذا في «التاتارخانية»، وخرجت بهذا القيد الشابة الممتدة الطهر ، فإنّ عدّتها ليست بالأشهر كما ذكرنا سابقاً.
(۲) قوله : ثلاثة أشهر ؛ لقوله في سورة الطلاق : ﴿وَالَّتِي يَئِسْنَ مِنَ الْمَحِيضِ مِن نِّسَائِكُمْ إِنِ ارْتَبْتُمْ فَعِدَّتُهُنَّ ثَلَاثَةُ أَشْهُرٍ وَالَّتِي لَمْ يَحِضْنَ﴾ . (۳) قوله : أربعة أشهر ؛ لقوله تعالى في سورة البقرة : ﴿وَالَّذِينَ يُتَوَفَّوْنَ مِنكُمْ وَيَذَرُونَ أَزْوَاجًا يَتَرَبَّصْنَ بِأَنفُسِهِنَّ أَرْبَعَةَ أَشْهُرٍ وَعَشْرًا﴾ . [٤]قوله : ولأمة ؛ أي زوجته أمة سواء كانت قنّة أو مدبّرة أو مكاتبة أو أم ولد لمولاها زوجها به، أو مستسعاة عند الإمام، ولا بُدّ هاهنا أيضاً من قيد الدخول لما مرّ من أنها لا عدة على الزوجة الغير المدخولة حقيقة أو حكماً ، وقيد بالزوجة؛ إذ لا عدة على الأمة بالوطء بملك اليمين إلا إذا كانت أم ولد ، كما مرّ.
[0] قوله : حيضتان ؛ لحديث: «عدّة الأمة حيضتان»، أخرجه أبو داود والترمذي وابن ماجة والحاكم وغيرهم، وقال عمر : لو استطعت أن أجعل عدة الأمة حيضة ونصفاً لفعلت ) ، أخرجه عبد الرزاق وابن أبي شَيْبة والبيهقي وغيرهم، وأشار به إلى أنّ تكميل الحيضة الثانية لعدم إمكان التجزؤ.
ومن لم تحض ، أو مات عنها زوجها نصف ما للحرّة ، وللحامل الحرة أو الأمة
ولمن لم تحض، أو مات عنها زوجها نصف ما للحرّة) : أي العدة لأمة تحيض للطلاق والفسخ حيضتان ، ولأمة لم تحض للطلاق والفسخ نصف ما للحرة، أي شهرٌ ونَصفُ شهر، وأما للموتِ فنصفُ ما للحرَّةِ أيضاً، وهو شهران وخمسة أيام.
[۲] قوله : وللحامل الحرة أو الأمة ، فإنَّه لا فرق في الحامل بين أن تكون حرة ، أو أمة
[١] قوله : نصف ما للحرّة ؛ فعدّة الحرّة في صورة كونها آيسة أو صغيرة أو بالغةً بالسن ثلاثة أشهر، فتكون العدّة لأمة كذلك شهر ونصف شهر، وعدة الحرّة في صورة موته أربعة أشهر وعشر فللأمة شهران وخمسة أيام، والوجه في كل ذلك أنّ الرق منصف للأحكام شرعاً. [٢] قوله : وللحامل ؛ أي العدّة للحامل مطلقاً هو وضع حملها، سواء كانت عن طلاق أو فسخ أو وفاة أو متاركة في النكاح الفاسد أو الوطء بشبهة، وسواء كانت حرّة أو أمة. كذا في «النهر». والوجه فيه إطلاق قوله تعالى : ﴿وَأُولَاتُ الْأَحْمَالِ أَجَلُهُنَّ أَن يَضَعْنَ حَمْلَهُنَّ﴾ ، وقد ورد في رواية البخاري ومسلم وغيرهما : «إنّ سبيعة الأسلمية وضعت حملها بعد وفاة زوجها بليال، فقال لها النبي ﷺ : انكحي من شئت) وفي «مصنف ابن أبي شَيْبَة» و «عبد الرزاق»: «إنّ أم كلثوم كانت تحت الزبير بن العوام فكرهته ، فسألته أن يطلقها وهي حامل فأبى، فلمّا ضربها الطلق ألحت عليه في تطليقه فطلقها واحدة وهو يتوضأ، ثم خرج فأدركه إنسان فأخبره أنها وضعت : فقال الزبير: خدعتني خدعها الله ، فأتى النبي ﷺ فذكر ذلك له، فقال: سبق كتاب الله فيها)
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Iddah. And for one who does not menstruate, three months; and for death, four months and ten days; and for a slave woman who menstruates, two menstrual periods. (And for one who does not menstruate, three months): meaning the 'iddah for a free woman who does not menstruate due to young age or similar for divorce and annulment is three months. (For death, four months and ten days): His saying: for death is conjunct to his saying: for divorce, and annulment, meaning the 'iddah for a free woman for death is four months and ten days. (And for a slave woman who menstruates, two menstrual periods). [1] His saying: and who does not menstruate; is related to his saying: she reaches maturity by age, and this includes one who did not see blood at all or saw it and it stopped before completion of the menstrual period, for if she saw blood for a day then it stopped until a year passed then he divorced her, her 'iddah is by months. Thus in al-TatarKhaniyya, and by this condition is excluded the young woman with extended purity, for her 'iddah is not by months as we mentioned earlier. (2) His saying: three months; due to His saying in Surah al-Talaq: And those who have despaired of menstruation among your women, if you doubt, their 'iddah is three months, and those who have not menstruated. (3) His saying: four months; due to His saying in Surah al-Baqarah: And those who are taken in death among you and leave wives, they shall wait by themselves four months and ten days. [4] His saying: and for a slave woman; meaning his wife is a slave woman, whether she is a qinn, mudabbara, mukataba, or umm walad for her master who is her husband, or musa'ah according to Imam; and here also the condition of consummation is necessary because of what has passed that there is no 'iddah for a wife not consummated actually or legally, and he restricted it with wife; for there is no 'iddah for a slave woman by intercourse with right hand of ownership unless she is an umm walad, as has passed. [0] His saying: two menstrual periods; due to the hadith: 'The 'iddah of a slave woman is two menstrual periods,' reported by Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah, al-Hakim and others; and 'Umar said: If I could, I would make the 'iddah of a slave woman one and a half periods, reported by 'Abd al-Razzaq, Ibn Abi Shayba, al-Bayhaqi and others; and he indicated by that that completing the second menstrual period is due to the impossibility of dividing. And for the one who does not menstruate, or whose husband dies on her, half of what is for a free woman. And for the pregnant woman, free or slave. (And for one who does not menstruate, or whose husband dies on her, half of what is for a free woman): meaning the 'iddah for a slave woman who menstruates for divorce and annulment is two menstrual periods; and for a slave woman who does not menstruate for divorce and annulment is half of what is for a free woman, i.e., one and a half months; and for death, half of what is for a free woman as well, i.e., two months and five days. [2] His saying: and for the pregnant woman, free or slave; for there is no difference in the pregnant woman between being free or slave. [1] His saying: half of what is for a free woman; for the 'iddah of a free woman in the case of being menopausal or young or reaching maturity by age is three months, so the 'iddah for a slave woman likewise is one and a half months; and the 'iddah of a free woman in the case of his death is four months and ten days, so for a slave woman it is two months and five days; and the reason in all that is that slavery halves the rulings legally. [2] His saying: and for the pregnant woman; meaning the 'iddah for a pregnant woman absolutely is giving birth to her pregnancy, whether it is due to divorce, annulment, death, or separation in an invalid marriage or intercourse with ambiguity, and whether she is free or slave. Thus in al-Nahr. And the reason is the absolute saying of Allah: And those with pregnancies, their term is that they give birth to their pregnancy; and it has come in a narration of al-Bukhari and Muslim and others: 'Subay'a al-Aslamiyya gave birth to her pregnancy a few nights after her husband's death, and the Prophet ﷺ said to her: Marry whomever you wish.' And in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba and 'Abd al-Razzaq: 'Umm Kulthum was under al-Zubayr ibn al-'Awwam and she disliked him, so she asked him to divorce her while she was pregnant, but he refused. When labor pains struck her, she insisted on his divorcing her, so he divorced her once while he was performing ablution. Then he went out and a person caught up with him and informed him that she had given birth. Al-Zubayr said: She tricked me, may Allah trick her. So he came to the Prophet ﷺ and mentioned that to him, and he said: The Book of Allah has preceded in her matter.'
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the 'iddah periods for free women and slave women in cases of divorce, annulment, and death. The author begins by stating the general rules: a free woman who does not menstruate (due to young age or old age) observes an 'iddah of three months; for death, the 'iddah is four months and ten days; a slave woman who menstruates observes two menstrual periods. The commentator then explains each part. The phrase 'and for one who does not menstruate' is connected to the earlier condition of reaching maturity by age, and it includes women who have never menstruated or whose menstruation stopped before completion. If a woman sees blood for a day then it stops for a year and then she is divorced, her 'iddah is by months. This exception excludes a young woman with extended purity whose 'iddah is not by months. The proof for three months is from Surah al-Talaq (65:4) regarding those who have despaired of menstruation and those who have not menstruated. The proof for four months and ten days is from Surah al-Baqarah (2:234). For a slave woman, the 'iddah of two menstrual periods is based on the hadith reported by several compilers. 'Umar's statement about wanting to make it one and a half periods indicates the completeness of two periods. The commentator then addresses the case of a slave woman who does not menstruate or whose husband dies: her 'iddah is half of that of a free woman—one and a half months for divorce, and two months and five days for death. The reason is that slavery halves legal rulings. Finally, for a pregnant woman, whether free or slave, her 'iddah ends with delivery, based on Surah al-Talaq (65:4) and the story of Subay'a al-Aslamiyya and Umm Kulthum. This is absolute and applies to all types of separation.

Key Terms
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband before she can remarry.
حيض (hayd): Menstruation, used to determine 'iddah for women who menstruate.
أمة (amah): A female slave, whose 'iddah is half that of a free woman in most cases.
طلاق (talaq): Divorce by the husband, one of the causes of 'iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the 'iddah for a slave woman whose husband dies, and she does not menstruate?
A) Four months and ten days
B) Two months and five days
C) Three months
D) One and a half months

Key Takeaway
The 'iddah periods are clearly prescribed by Quran and Sunnah, with slavery halving the duration for slave women, and pregnancy ending the 'iddah at delivery.

Talaq — Divorce

Slave and Pregnant Women's 'Iddah in Hanafi Fiqh
Explanation of 'iddah for slave and pregnant women with Quranic and Prophetic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the 'iddah durations for slave women and explain the general ruling that a pregnant woman's 'iddah ends with delivery, and identify the evidence from Quran and Sunnah.

Core Idea
Slavery halves the 'iddah; pregnancy abrogates all other waiting periods.

This lesson covers two foundational 'iddah rulings. First, the effect of slavery on 'iddah durations: the waiting period for a slave woman is exactly half that of a free woman. Second, the overriding principle that a pregnant woman's 'iddah, regardless of her status, ends with delivery—based on the clear text of the Quran and confirmed by the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify free woman's 'iddah durations
Determine the 'iddah for a free woman in two scenarios: divorced (three months if not expecting menses) and widowed (four months ten days).
These are the base numbers for comparison.
2
Halve for slave woman
Apply the principle that slavery halves legal rulings. For divorce, the slave's 'iddah is one and a half months; for death, two months and five days.
This applies to both menstruating and non-menstruating slaves.
3
State the pregnant woman's rule
Any pregnant woman, free or slave, ends her 'iddah with delivery, regardless of the cause of separation.
This overrides the standard durations.
4
Identify the evidence
Cite Quran 65:4 and the hadith of Subay'ah al-Aslamiyyah, which show that delivery terminates the waiting period.
The story of Umm Kulthum also confirms this.
5
Note the consensus
These rules are agreed upon within the Hanafi school and generally accepted across schools for non-minor husbands.
The dispute on minor husbands is covered in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب العدة
ومن لم تحض، أو مات عنها زوجها نصف ما للحرّة، وللحامل الحرة أو الأمة ولمن لم تحض، أو مات عنها زوجها نصف ما للحرّة: أي العدة لأمة تحيض للطلاق والفسخ حيضتان، ولأمة لم تحض للطلاق والفسخ نصف ما للحرة، أي شهرٌ ونَصفُ شهر، وأما للموتِ فنصفُ ما للحرَّةِ أيضاً، وهو شهران وخمسة أيام.
[١] قوله: نصف ما للحرّة؛ فعدّة الحرّة في صورة كونها آيسة أو صغيرة أو بالغةً بالسن ثلاثة أشهر، فتكون العدّة لأمة كذلك شهر ونصف شهر، وعدة الحرّة في صورة موته أربعة أشهر وعشر فللأمة شهران وخمسة أيام، والوجه في كل ذلك أنّ الرق منصف للأحكام شرعاً.
[٢] قوله: وللحامل؛ أي العدّة للحامل مطلقاً هو وضع حملها، سواء كانت عن طلاق أو فسخ أو وفاة أو متاركة في النكاح الفاسد أو الوطء بشبهة، وسواء كانت حرّة أو أمة. كذا في «النهر». والوجه فيه إطلاق قوله: ﴿وَأُولَتُ الْأَعْمَالِ أَجَلُهُنَّ أَن يَضَعْنَ حَمَلَهُنَّ﴾.
وقد ورد في رواية البخاري ومسلم وغيرهما: «إنّ سبيعة الأسلمية وضعت حملها بعد وفاة زوجها بليال، فقال لها النبي ﷺ: انكحي من شئت».
وفي «مصنف ابن أبي شَيْبَة» و «عبد الرزاق»: «إنّ أم كلثوم كانت تحت الزبير بن العوام فكرهته، فسألته أن يطلقها وهي حامل فأبى، فلمّا ضربها الطلق ألحت عليه في تطليقه فطلقها واحدة وهو يتوضأ، ثم خرج فأدركه إنسان فأخبره أنها وضعت: فقال الزبير: خدعتني خدعها الله، فأتى النبي ﷺ فذكر ذلك له، فقال: سبق كتاب الله فيها».
(وإن مات عنها صبي بوضع الحمل).
[١] قوله: وضع حملها؛ أي بجميع أجزائها، فلو خرج أكثـر الـولـد لـم تـصـح الرجعة، وحلّت للأزواج، وقالا أكثرهم لا تحلّ احتياطاً، والمراد بالحمل الذي استبان بعض خلقه أو كلّه، فإن لم يستبن لم تنقض العدة. كذا في «البحر».
[٢] قوله: عدتها؛ يعني عدة الحامل التي زوجها صبي، وقد مات عنها أربعة أشهر وعشر، لا بوضع الحمل؛ لأنّ الحمل ليس بثابت النسب منه، فصار وجوده وعدمه سواء، فصار كالحادث بعد الموت، بأن تضع بعد الموتِ بستة أشهر فصاعداً من يوم الموت، وقال بعضُهم: بأن يأتي لأكثر من سنتين، والأول أصح. كذا في «النهاية».
وعند أبي يوسف والشَّافِعِي عدتها عدة الوفاة؛ لأن العدةَ بوضع الحمل إنما تجب لصيانة الماء، وذلك في ثابت النسب، وهنا لا يثبتُ النَّسبُ عَنْ الصبي.
ولأبي حنيفة ومحمد أن قوله تعالى: ﴿وَأُوْلَتُ الْأَعْمَالِ أَجَلُهُنَّ أَن يَضَعْنَ حَمْلَهُنَّ﴾ نَزَل بعد قوله تعالى: ﴿وَالَّذِينَ يُتَوَفَّوْنَ مِنكُمْ وَيَذَرُونَ أَزْوَاجًا يَتَرَبَّصْنَ بِأَنفُسِهِنَّ أَرْبَعَةَ أَشْهُرٍ وَعَشْرًا﴾.
Book of Divorce, Chapter of 'Iddah. And she who does not menstruate, or whose husband dies, (her 'iddah is) half of what is for a free woman. And for a pregnant woman, free or slave, and for she who does not menstruate, or whose husband dies, half of what is for a free woman: i.e., the 'iddah for a slave woman who menstruates for divorce and annulment is two menstruations, and for a slave woman who does not menstruate for divorce and annulment is half of what is for a free woman, i.e., one month and a half. And as for death, it is half of what is for a free woman as well, i.e., two months and five days. [1] His saying: half of what is for a free woman; so the 'iddah of a free woman in the case of being menopausal or young or an adult by age is three months, so the 'iddah of a slave woman likewise is one and a half months, and the 'iddah of a free woman in the case of his death is four months and ten days, so for the slave woman it is two months and five days, and the consideration in all that is that slavery halves rulings legally. [2] His saying: and for a pregnant woman; i.e., the 'iddah for a pregnant woman absolutely is the delivery of her pregnancy, whether it be from divorce, annulment, death, or separation in an invalid marriage, or intercourse with a misgivings, and whether she is free or a slave. Thus in al-Nahr. And the consideration is the general meaning of His statement: And those who are pregnant, their appointed term is that they give birth to their burden. And it has come in the narration of al-Bukhari and Muslim and others: 'Indeed Subay'ah al-Aslamiyyah gave birth to her pregnancy a few nights after the death of her husband, and the Prophet said to her: Marry whomever you wish.' And in the Musannaf of Ibn Abi Shaybah and Abd al-Razzaq: 'Indeed Umm Kulthum was under al-Zubayr ibn al-Awwam and she disliked him, so she asked him to divorce her while she was pregnant and he refused. When labor pains struck her, she insisted on his divorcing her, so he divorced her once while he was performing ablution. Then he went out and someone caught up with him and informed him that she had given birth. So al-Zubayr said: She deceived me, may God deceive her. Then he came to the Prophet and mentioned that to him, and he said: The Book of God has preceded her.' (And if a minor dies upon her, she (ends her 'iddah) by delivery of her pregnancy.) [1] His saying: delivery of her pregnancy; i.e., with all its parts. So if most of the child emerges, the revocability is not valid, and she becomes permissible for (other) husbands. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?) say: most of it, she does not become permissible, out of precaution. And the intended meaning by pregnancy is that whose creation has become distinct either partially or completely, for if it has not become distinct, the 'iddah is not concluded. Thus in al-Bahr. [2] His saying: her 'iddah; meaning the 'iddah of a pregnant woman whose husband is a minor and has died is four months and ten days, not by delivery of the pregnancy, because the pregnancy is not with established lineage from him, so its existence and non-existence are equal, so it becomes like a new occurrence after death, i.e., if she gives birth after death six months or more from the day of death. And some said: it may come after more than two years, and the first is more correct. Thus in al-Nihayah. And according to Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, her 'iddah is the 'iddah of widowhood, because the 'iddah by delivery of pregnancy is only required for the preservation of the sperm, and that (applies) when lineage is established, and here lineage is not established from the minor. And for Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, indeed Allah's statement: And those who are pregnant, their appointed term is that they give birth to their burden was revealed after His statement: And those who are taken among you and leave wives, they shall wait by themselves for four months and ten.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on al-Wiqayah, expanding on the rulings of 'iddah for slave women and pregnant women. The author first explains that the 'iddah of a slave woman who does not menstruate or whose husband dies is half that of a free woman: for a free woman who is menopausal, too young, or an adult by age, the 'iddah is three months; for the slave, it is one and a half months. For the death case, the free woman waits four months and ten days, the slave waits half that: two months and five days. The principle is that slavery halves legal rulings. Next, the author states that for any pregnant woman, whether free or slave, regardless of the cause (divorce, death, etc.), her 'iddah ends with delivery. This is based on the Quranic verse (65:4) and supported by hadith of Subay'ah and the story of Umm Kulthum. Then a dispute is presented: if the husband is a minor (underage) and dies, what is the 'iddah? Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i say it is the full 'iddah of widowhood (four months ten days) because the pregnancy does not establish lineage from the minor, so it is not considered a valid pregnancy for 'iddah. The reason for 'iddah by delivery is to preserve the sperm and establish lineage, which is absent here. However, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that the verse on pregnant women (65:4) was revealed after the verse on widow's 'iddah (2:234), so it abrogates it for pregnant women. The general rule applies regardless of the husband's age or whether lineage is established. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: the 'iddah of a pregnant woman always ends with delivery, even if the husband is a minor. The commentators also note that the delivery must be of a fully formed child (all parts or most) for the 'iddah to end; if the child is not yet formed, the 'iddah continues.

Key Terms
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce, annulment, or death of husband before remarrying.
أمة (amah): A female slave.
صبي (sabi): A male child who has not reached the age of puberty; here specifically a husband who is a minor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the 'iddah of a slave woman who is widowed?
A) Three months
B) Four months ten days
C) Two months and five days
D) One and a half months

Key Takeaway
The 'iddah of a slave is half that of a free woman, and any pregnant woman's 'iddah ends with delivery, based on clear Quranic and Prophetic evidence.

The 'Iddah of a Pregnant Woman Whose Husband is a Minor: Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the 'iddah of a pregnant woman whose husband is a minor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the 'iddah of a pregnant woman whose minor husband dies, and evaluate the arguments based on Quranic abrogation versus the purpose of 'iddah.

Core Idea
The verse on pregnant women abrogates the widow's 'iddah; lineage establishment is irrelevant.

This lesson focuses on a specific intra-Hanafi dispute: if a woman's husband is a minor (pre-pubescent) and dies while she is pregnant, does her 'iddah end with delivery or must she observe the full four months ten days? Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i argue that delivery ends 'iddah only when lineage can be established, which is not possible with a minor. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad insist on the general applicability of the Quranic verse, which abrogated the widow's waiting period for all pregnant women.

Guided Path
1
State the case
A woman's husband dies, and her husband was a minor (pre-pubescent). She is pregnant.
The key question: does the pregnancy reduce her 'iddah to delivery, or does she wait the full four months ten days?
2
Present Abu Yusuf's position
According to Abu Yusuf (and al-Shafi'i), her 'iddah is the full widow's 'iddah of four months ten days, not ending with delivery.
Reason: delivery-based 'iddah exists to preserve sperm and establish lineage; a minor's sperm cannot establish lineage, so the pregnancy does not serve its purpose.
3
Present Abu Hanifa's position
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, her 'iddah ends with delivery.
They argue that Quran 65:4 is unconditional and was revealed after the widow's verse, abrogating it for all pregnant women.
4
Analyze the arguments
Compare the textual evidence (unconditional verse) with the purposive reasoning (preservation of sperm).
Which carries more weight: clear text or underlying rationale?
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: 'iddah ends with delivery.
This is based on the precedence of the clear text of the Quran.

كتاب الطلاق باب العدة
ومن لم تحض، أو مات عنها زوجها نصف ما للحرّة، وللحامل الحرة أو الأمة ولمن لم تحض، أو مات عنها زوجها نصف ما للحرّة: أي العدة لأمة تحيض للطلاق والفسخ حيضتان، ولأمة لم تحض للطلاق والفسخ نصف ما للحرة، أي شهرٌ ونَصفُ شهر، وأما للموتِ فنصفُ ما للحرَّةِ أيضاً، وهو شهران وخمسة أيام.
[١] قوله: نصف ما للحرّة؛ فعدّة الحرّة في صورة كونها آيسة أو صغيرة أو بالغةً بالسن ثلاثة أشهر، فتكون العدّة لأمة كذلك شهر ونصف شهر، وعدة الحرّة في صورة موته أربعة أشهر وعشر فللأمة شهران وخمسة أيام، والوجه في كل ذلك أنّ الرق منصف للأحكام شرعاً.
[٢] قوله: وللحامل؛ أي العدّة للحامل مطلقاً هو وضع حملها، سواء كانت عن طلاق أو فسخ أو وفاة أو متاركة في النكاح الفاسد أو الوطء بشبهة، وسواء كانت حرّة أو أمة. كذا في «النهر». والوجه فيه إطلاق قوله: ﴿وَأُولَتُ الْأَعْمَالِ أَجَلُهُنَّ أَن يَضَعْنَ حَمَلَهُنَّ﴾.
وقد ورد في رواية البخاري ومسلم وغيرهما: «إنّ سبيعة الأسلمية وضعت حملها بعد وفاة زوجها بليال، فقال لها النبي ﷺ: انكحي من شئت».
وفي «مصنف ابن أبي شَيْبَة» و «عبد الرزاق»: «إنّ أم كلثوم كانت تحت الزبير بن العوام فكرهته، فسألته أن يطلقها وهي حامل فأبى، فلمّا ضربها الطلق ألحت عليه في تطليقه فطلقها واحدة وهو يتوضأ، ثم خرج فأدركه إنسان فأخبره أنها وضعت: فقال الزبير: خدعتني خدعها الله، فأتى النبي ﷺ فذكر ذلك له، فقال: سبق كتاب الله فيها».
(وإن مات عنها صبي بوضع الحمل).
[١] قوله: وضع حملها؛ أي بجميع أجزائها، فلو خرج أكثـر الـولـد لـم تـصـح الرجعة، وحلّت للأزواج، وقالا أكثرهم لا تحلّ احتياطاً، والمراد بالحمل الذي استبان بعض خلقه أو كلّه، فإن لم يستبن لم تنقض العدة. كذا في «البحر».
[٢] قوله: عدتها؛ يعني عدة الحامل التي زوجها صبي، وقد مات عنها أربعة أشهر وعشر، لا بوضع الحمل؛ لأنّ الحمل ليس بثابت النسب منه، فصار وجوده وعدمه سواء، فصار كالحادث بعد الموت، بأن تضع بعد الموتِ بستة أشهر فصاعداً من يوم الموت، وقال بعضُهم: بأن يأتي لأكثر من سنتين، والأول أصح. كذا في «النهاية».
وعند أبي يوسف والشَّافِعِي عدتها عدة الوفاة؛ لأن العدةَ بوضع الحمل إنما تجب لصيانة الماء، وذلك في ثابت النسب، وهنا لا يثبتُ النَّسبُ عَنْ الصبي.
ولأبي حنيفة ومحمد أن قوله تعالى: ﴿وَأُوْلَتُ الْأَعْمَالِ أَجَلُهُنَّ أَن يَضَعْنَ حَمْلَهُنَّ﴾ نَزَل بعد قوله تعالى: ﴿وَالَّذِينَ يُتَوَفَّوْنَ مِنكُمْ وَيَذَرُونَ أَزْوَاجًا يَتَرَبَّصْنَ بِأَنفُسِهِنَّ أَرْبَعَةَ أَشْهُرٍ وَعَشْرًا﴾.
Book of Divorce, Chapter of 'Iddah. And she who does not menstruate, or whose husband dies, (her 'iddah is) half of what is for a free woman. And for a pregnant woman, free or slave, and for she who does not menstruate, or whose husband dies, half of what is for a free woman: i.e., the 'iddah for a slave woman who menstruates for divorce and annulment is two menstruations, and for a slave woman who does not menstruate for divorce and annulment is half of what is for a free woman, i.e., one month and a half. And as for death, it is half of what is for a free woman as well, i.e., two months and five days. [1] His saying: half of what is for a free woman; so the 'iddah of a free woman in the case of being menopausal or young or an adult by age is three months, so the 'iddah of a slave woman likewise is one and a half months, and the 'iddah of a free woman in the case of his death is four months and ten days, so for the slave woman it is two months and five days, and the consideration in all that is that slavery halves rulings legally. [2] His saying: and for a pregnant woman; i.e., the 'iddah for a pregnant woman absolutely is the delivery of her pregnancy, whether it be from divorce, annulment, death, or separation in an invalid marriage, or intercourse with a misgivings, and whether she is free or a slave. Thus in al-Nahr. And the consideration is the general meaning of His statement: And those who are pregnant, their appointed term is that they give birth to their burden. And it has come in the narration of al-Bukhari and Muslim and others: 'Indeed Subay'ah al-Aslamiyyah gave birth to her pregnancy a few nights after the death of her husband, and the Prophet said to her: Marry whomever you wish.' And in the Musannaf of Ibn Abi Shaybah and Abd al-Razzaq: 'Indeed Umm Kulthum was under al-Zubayr ibn al-Awwam and she disliked him, so she asked him to divorce her while she was pregnant and he refused. When labor pains struck her, she insisted on his divorcing her, so he divorced her once while he was performing ablution. Then he went out and someone caught up with him and informed him that she had given birth. So al-Zubayr said: She deceived me, may God deceive her. Then he came to the Prophet and mentioned that to him, and he said: The Book of God has preceded her.' (And if a minor dies upon her, she (ends her 'iddah) by delivery of her pregnancy.) [1] His saying: delivery of her pregnancy; i.e., with all its parts. So if most of the child emerges, the revocability is not valid, and she becomes permissible for (other) husbands. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?) say: most of it, she does not become permissible, out of precaution. And the intended meaning by pregnancy is that whose creation has become distinct either partially or completely, for if it has not become distinct, the 'iddah is not concluded. Thus in al-Bahr. [2] His saying: her 'iddah; meaning the 'iddah of a pregnant woman whose husband is a minor and has died is four months and ten days, not by delivery of the pregnancy, because the pregnancy is not with established lineage from him, so its existence and non-existence are equal, so it becomes like a new occurrence after death, i.e., if she gives birth after death six months or more from the day of death. And some said: it may come after more than two years, and the first is more correct. Thus in al-Nihayah. And according to Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, her 'iddah is the 'iddah of widowhood, because the 'iddah by delivery of pregnancy is only required for the preservation of the sperm, and that (applies) when lineage is established, and here lineage is not established from the minor. And for Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, indeed Allah's statement: And those who are pregnant, their appointed term is that they give birth to their burden was revealed after His statement: And those who are taken among you and leave wives, they shall wait by themselves for four months and ten.

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on al-Wiqayah, expanding on the rulings of 'iddah for slave women and pregnant women. The author first explains that the 'iddah of a slave woman who does not menstruate or whose husband dies is half that of a free woman: for a free woman who is menopausal, too young, or an adult by age, the 'iddah is three months; for the slave, it is one and a half months. The commentators also note that the delivery must be of a fully formed child (all parts or most) for the 'iddah to end; if the child is not yet formed, the 'iddah continues.

صبي (sabi): A male child who has not reached the age of puberty; here specifically a husband who is a minor.
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation of an earlier ruling by a later revelation.

Iddah of a Pregnant Widow Whose Husband Was a Minor
Ruling on 'iddah of pregnant woman whose deceased husband is a minor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the 'iddah of a pregnant widow when the deceased husband is a minor, explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement, and apply the ruling to similar cases.

Core Idea
When a minor husband dies, his widow's 'iddah is four months and ten days, not by delivery, because the pregnancy is not attributed to him.

The death of a husband imposes a waiting period ('iddah) on the widow. But what if the husband was a minor? This lesson examines a key disagreement within the Hanafi school about whether such a widow's 'iddah ends with childbirth or remains the standard four months and ten days. The answer turns on whether the pregnancy can be legally attributed to the minor husband.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two Hanafi positions
State the views of Abu Hanifa & Muhammad vs. Abu Yusuf on the 'iddah of a pregnant widow whose deceased husband was a minor.
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the 'iddah is four months ten days (not by delivery); Abu Yusuf holds the same result but with different reasoning (the 'iddah of death).
2
Understand the basis for each position
For Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the 'iddah by delivery (via naskh of 2:234 by 65:4) requires the pregnancy to be attributed to the husband; since a minor cannot establish lineage, the general rule of four months ten days applies. For Abu Yusuf, the 'iddah of death applies regardless because the pregnancy is not protected.
The key difference is the application of naskh: does the verse on delivery abrogate the verse on four months for all pregnant widows, or only when the pregnancy is attributed?
3
Examine the Quranic evidence and naskh
The verse in Surah al-Talaq (65:4) says pregnant women's term is delivery. The verse in Surah al-Baqarah (2:234) says widows wait four months ten days. The later revelation of 65:4 abrogates 2:234 in the overlapping case of a pregnant widow, but only if the pregnancy is attributed to the deceased husband.
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the abrogation is limited to cases where the pregnancy is established from the husband.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: four months and ten days for a minor husband. This is stated as the correct view by the commentator.
The practical ruling: even if she delivers earlier, she must wait the full four months and ten days.

And if a minor dies while she is pregnant, her 'iddah is by delivery of her pregnancy. (3) According to Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, her 'iddah is the 'iddah of death, because the 'iddah by delivery of pregnancy is only required for the protection of the sperm, and that is in the case of established lineage, and here the lineage is not established from the minor. (0) According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'And those who are pregnant, their term is when they deliver their pregnancy' was revealed after His saying: 'And those among you who die and leave wives, they shall wait for themselves four months and ten days.' [1] His saying: 'delivery of her pregnancy': i.e., by all its parts. If most of the child comes out, the revocation does not become valid, and she becomes permissible for husbands; and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: most does not make her permissible out of caution. And what is meant by pregnancy is that which has become clear in part or in whole of its creation; if it has not become clear, the 'iddah is not ended. Thus in 'al-Bahr'. [2] His saying: 'her 'iddah': meaning the 'iddah of the pregnant woman whose husband is a minor, and he died; it is four months and ten days, not by delivery, because the pregnancy is not of established lineage from him, so its existence and non-existence are equal, so it becomes like something happening after death, such as she gives birth after death by six months or more from the day of death. Some said: that it must be more than two years; the first is more correct. Thus in 'al-Nihaya'. [3] His saying: 'revealed... etc.': The commentator said in 'al-Tawdih' in the chapter on the general: 'Ali and Ibn Mas'ud differed concerning a pregnant woman whose husband died. 'Ali said: She observes the 'iddah of the longer of the two periods, reconciling the two verses: one in Surah al-Baqarah, which is His saying: 'And those among you who die... etc.' and the other in Surah al-Nisa' al-Qusra, which is His saying: 'And those who are pregnant... etc.' Ibn Mas'ud said: Whoever wishes, I will swear that Surah al-Nisa' al-Qusra was revealed after Surah al-Nisa' al-Tula, and His saying: 'And those who are pregnant' was revealed after His saying: 'And those who die...' So His saying: 'they shall wait' indicates that the 'iddah of a woman whose husband has died is by months, whether she is pregnant or not. And His saying: 'And those who are pregnant' indicates that the 'iddah of a pregnant woman is by delivery, whether her husband died or divorced her. So He made His saying: 'And those who are pregnant' abrogating for His saying: 'they shall wait' to the extent that the two verses cover each other, which is when her husband has died and she is pregnant.' End. [1] His saying: 'to the extent that the two verses cover each other... etc.': That is because 'those who are pregnant' does not include a woman whose husband has died and who is not pregnant, and His saying: 'And those who die' i.e., the wives of those who die among you, does not include a pregnant divorced woman. So His saying: 'And those who are pregnant' with regard to establishing the 'iddah of a pregnant divorced woman, is not abrogating because it is not included under the verse of al-Baqarah; and His saying: 'And those who die' with regard to imposing the 'iddah of a woman whose husband died and who is not pregnant, is not abrogated because it is not included under the verse of Surah al-Talaq. Abrogation only occurs in the extent that both verses include, which is the pregnant woman whose husband died, so her 'iddah is by delivery, not by months.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage discusses the 'iddah (waiting period) of a pregnant widow when the deceased husband was a minor (صبي). The central issue is whether her 'iddah ends with the delivery of the child (وضع الحمل) or is the standard four months and ten days for widows. The author first states the ruling from the matn: 'If a minor dies while she is pregnant, her 'iddah is by delivery of her pregnancy.' However, the commentary clarifies that this applies only if the pregnancy is attributable to the deceased husband with established lineage. If the husband was a minor (non-pubescent), the lineage from him is not established, so the 'iddah becomes four months and ten days according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, while Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i hold that it is the 'iddah of death (four months ten days) regardless. The proof for Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is the abrogation (naskh) of the verse from Surah al-Baqarah (four months ten days for widows) by the verse from Surah al-Talaq (delivery for pregnant women), but only in the case where the widow is pregnant. The passage then delves into the concept of valid delivery: the child must have exited entirely or mostly, and must have shown some formation (استبان خلقه). If the child is not formed, the 'iddah does not end. For the minor husband, since the pregnancy cannot be attributed to him, the 'iddah remains four months and ten days. The passage also includes a discussion of the naskh reasoning between the two verses, citing the views of Ali and Ibn Mas'ud, showing how abrogation is limited to the overlapping case of a pregnant widow.

Key Terms
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after the death of her husband or divorce before remarriage.
صبي (sabiyy): A minor, non-pubescent boy. In this context, a husband who is not yet baligh (mature).
ضع حمل (wada' haml): To deliver the pregnancy; the act of giving birth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Until she delivers the child.
B) Four months and ten days.
C) Three menstrual cycles.
D) The longer of the two periods (delivery or four months ten days).

Key Takeaway
A pregnant widow's 'iddah ends with delivery only if the pregnancy is attributed to the deceased husband; if the husband was a minor, the 'iddah is four months and ten days.

Iddah of Pregnant Widow and Naskh of Verses
Discussion on naskh between Quranic verses and ruling for pregnant widow's iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the iddah of a pregnant widow and explain the naskh between the two Quranic verses.

Core Idea
A pregnant widow's iddah ends with delivery, not four months, because Surah al-Talaq abrogates the earlier verse in the overlapping case.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a key issue in Hanafi fiqh: how to reconcile two seemingly contradictory Quranic verses regarding the iddah of a widow. The ruling has direct implications for a pregnant widow, and the method of naskh is used to resolve the apparent conflict. This lesson will guide you through the legal reasoning and the Hanafi response to a specific objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two Quranic verses
The first verse (2:240) prescribes a four-month-and-ten-day iddah for widows. The second verse (65:4) states that the iddah of pregnant women ends with delivery. Identify which verse applies to which scenario.
The second verse is from Surah al-Talaq, also known as Surat al-Nisa' al-Qusra.
2
Understand the principle of naskh
The author establishes, based on a report from Ibn Mas'ud, that the second verse was revealed later and thus abrogates the first, but only in the overlapping case of a pregnant widow. Non-pregnant widows remain under the four-month rule.
Naskh is valid when the later verse explicitly or implicitly supersedes the earlier.
3
Define the scope of the verses
The verse from al-Baqarah covers all widows (2:240) but does not include divorcees. The verse from al-Talaq covers all pregnant women in iddah, including both widows and divorcees. The abrogation occurs only where both verses apply: a pregnant widow.
A pregnant divorcee is covered only by the second verse, so no abrogation is needed.
4
Analyze the objection regarding the minor's widow
An objector argues that the verse from al-Talaq only applies when the pregnancy's lineage is established from the deceased husband. The widow of a minor (who is incapable of fatherhood) would thus not be covered. The Hanafi response rejects this restriction.
The purpose of iddah is to ascertain the purity of the womb, which is achieved by delivery regardless of lineage.
5
Apply the ruling to a post-death pregnancy
If a woman becomes pregnant after the death of her husband (e.g., the minor's widow gives birth six months or more after death), the iddah was fixed at the moment of death, so she must observe the four-month-and-ten-day iddah, not the delivery-based one.
The iddah is determined by the state at the time of death; subsequent pregnancy does not change it.

And this general meaning is found in every pregnancy existing at the time of the husband's death, whether its lineage from him is established or not, or in another way. The waiting period of death was legislated to fulfill the right of marriage; for the non-pregnant it is by months, and for the pregnant it is by delivery. For then it is not certain that the pregnancy existed at the time of his death; thus she must observe the waiting period by months to fulfill the right of marriage, and it does not change with the occurrence of pregnancy, unlike the previous case, for there, as soon as the waiting period was obligatory, it was determined.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a ruling from Al-Hidayah regarding the iddah (waiting period) of a pregnant widow. It begins by establishing the principle of naskh (abrogation) between two Quranic verses: Surah al-Baqarah (2:240), which prescribes a four-month-and-ten-day iddah for widows, and Surah al-Talaq (65:4), which states that the iddah of pregnant women ends with delivery. The author, following the report of Ibn Mas'ud, holds that Surah al-Talaq was revealed later and thus abrogates Surah al-Baqarah, but only in the overlapping case: a pregnant widow. For a non-pregnant widow, the original four-month rule remains; for a pregnant divorcee, Surah al-Talaq applies without abrogation because she was never included in the widow's verse. The commentary then addresses an objection: that the phrase 'those who are pregnant' in Surah al-Talaq refers only to women whose pregnancy's lineage is established from the husband. The objector argues that the widow of a minor (a husband who has not reached puberty) cannot have a pregnancy traced to him, so she should not benefit from the ruling of delivery. The Hanafi response is firm: 'We do not accept (la nusallim).' The purpose of iddah is to determine the purity of the womb (bara'at al-rahim), which is achieved by delivery regardless of lineage. Therefore, any pregnant woman upon whom iddah is obligatory—whether the pregnancy is from the husband or not—ends her iddah with delivery. The commentary then gives a separate ruling: if a woman becomes pregnant after the death of her minor husband (by giving birth six months or more after his death), she must observe the iddah of death (four months and ten days) because she was not pregnant at the time of death. The iddah was fixed at the moment of death, and a subsequent pregnancy does not change it. This distinction clarifies the importance of timing: the iddah is determined by the state at the time of the husband's death, not by later events.

Key Terms
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after the death of her husband or divorce, during which she cannot remarry.
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation of a legal ruling in one Quranic verse by a later revealed verse.
أولات الأحمال (ulat al-ahmal): Pregnant women (literally 'those possessing loads/burdens').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The iddah of a pregnant widow ends with delivery, based on the later revealed verse of Surah al-Talaq abrogating the four-month rule in the specific case.

Iddah of a Woman Pregnant After Her Minor Husband's Death
Ruling with proof on iddah after minor's death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the iddah of a woman who becomes pregnant after her minor husband's death and explain the reasoning based on the Quranic verses.

Core Idea
A widow's iddah is by delivery only if pregnancy existed at death; otherwise, it is by months.

When a minor husband dies and his wife later becomes pregnant, the Hanafi school rules that she observes the iddah of death (four months and ten days), not the iddah of delivery. This is because the pregnancy was not present at the moment of death. The passage defends this ruling against an objection that the Quranic verse about pregnant women (Surah al-Talaq) should apply to all pregnancies. We will explore the proof and the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Determine the iddah for a woman whose minor husband dies and she later becomes pregnant (delivery after at least six months). The ruling is iddat al-wafat (four months and ten days).
This applies when pregnancy began after death.
2
Understand the objection
An objector argues that the Quranic verse in Surah al-Talaq (65:4) says pregnant women's iddah ends with delivery, and this should include all pregnant widows regardless of when pregnancy began.
The objection rests on the generality of the phrase 'wa ulaatu al-ahmal' (and those with pregnancies).
3
Trace the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds by restricting the verse to pregnancies existing at the time of death. If pregnancy began after death, the verse on months (Surah al-Baqara 2:234) applies, because the purpose of iddah (knowing the emptiness of the womb) is already achieved by delivery, but the timing of the pregnancy determines which legal text governs.
Hanafi usul: the general command is restricted by the context of certainty of pregnancy at death.
4
Identify the purpose of iddah
The purpose of iddah is to ascertain that the womb is empty. Delivery fulfills this, but the rule is not universal—it applies only when pregnancy is certain at death.
In the case of a minor husband, the pregnancy cannot be attributed to him, but the iddah rule still protects against confusion of lineage.
5
Summarize the conclusion
Since the pregnancy was not certain at death, the wife observes iddah by months. This ruling is specific to the Hanafi school and differs from the Shafi'i view.
The practical result: the iddah is fixed at four months ten days, not variable until delivery.

ولمن حبلت ( بعد موتِ الصَّبي عدة الموت)؛ لأنها لم تكن حاملاً وقت موت الصبى تُعينَ عدَّة الموت. فإن قيل: المراد أولاتُ الأحمال اللاتي ثبت نسب حملهن. قلنا: لا نسلّم بل أولاتُ الأحمال اللاتي وجبَتْ عليهنَّ العدة، فعدتُهنَّ أن يضعن حملهن. وحاصله: إنَّ هذا القدر لا يثبتُ المطلوب الذي نحن بصدد إثباته، فإنّ المراد في آية سورة الطلاق من وَأُولَتُ الأعمال الحوامل اللاتي يثبت نسب حملهن من زوجهن المتوفى، وزوجة الصبي المتوفى الحامل ليست كذلك، فإنّ نسب حملها غير ثابت من زوجها؛ لكونه غير قابل له، فلا تكون عدتها بوضع الحمل؛ لعدم اندراجها تحت آية سورة الطلاق تكون بالأشهر؛ لدخولها تحت آية الأشهر. حاصل الجواب: أنا لا نسلم أن المراد في آية الحمل الحوامل اللاتي يثبت نسب حملهن، كيف ولا يدل دليل على هذا التقييد، بل المراد مطلق الحوامل التي وجبت عليهن العدة، كيف لا والغرض من العدة تعرف براءة الرحم، وهي تحصل بوضع الحمل في الحامل، فلذلك جعلت عدتها وضع الحمل. وهذا عام يوجد في كل حمل موجود عند موت الزوج، سواء ثبت نسبه منه أو لم يثبت أو بوجه آخر عدة الوفاة شرعت لقضاء حق النكاح في غير الحامل بالأشهر، وفي الحامل بوضع الحمل، وهذا المعنى يتحقق في الصبي وإن لم يكن الحمل منه. ومن حبلت؛ أي تعتد امرأةُ الصبي المتوفى عنها إن حبلت بعد موته عدة الوفاة، وذلك بأن تلد لنصف سنة: أي ستة أشهر فأكثر، فإنّه حينئذ لا يتيقن بوجود الحمل حالِ موته فيجب عليها العدّة بالشهور قضاء لحق النكاح، ولا تتغير بحدوث الحمل، بخلاف الصورة السابقة، فإنّ هناك كما وجبت العدة وجبت مقدّرة بالحمل؛ لتيقن وجوده عند ذلك. ولا نسب في وجهيه: أي لا يثبت نسب ذلك الحمل من الزوج الصبي الميت في الصورتين؛ أي فيما إذا كان الحمل قائماً عند موت الصغير، وفيما إذا كان حادثاً بعد موته؛ لأنّ الصبي لا ماء له، فلا يتصوّر منه العلوق، وثبوت النسب فرع امكانه.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof discussing the iddah of a woman whose minor husband dies and she later becomes pregnant. The text establishes that such a woman observes the iddah of death (four months and ten days) rather than iddah by delivery, because she was not pregnant at the moment of death. The author anticipates an objection: the Quranic verse (Surah al-Talaq) regarding pregnant women refers only to those whose pregnancy's lineage is established from the deceased husband, which does not apply to the wife of a minor since he cannot father a child. The author refutes this restriction, arguing that the purpose of iddah is to determine the emptiness of the womb, and delivery achieves that regardless of lineage. However, because in this case the pregnancy is not known to have existed at the time of death (she gives birth six months or more after death), her iddah is by months, not delivery. The passage also clarifies that in both scenarios (pregnancy present at death or after), the child's lineage is not attributed to the minor husband, as a minor is incapable of procreation.

Key Terms
عدة الموت (iddat al-wafat): The waiting period observed by a widow: four months and ten days.
الصبي (al-sabi): A minor boy who is not yet capable of procreation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that a widow observes iddat al-wafat by months if she becomes pregnant after her husband's death, not by delivery.

Talaq — Divorce

The iddah of a woman divorced by a terminally ill husband: the longer of the two terms
Intra-Hanafi dispute on iddah for divorced woman of terminally ill husband

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the iddah of a woman divorced by a terminally ill husband and state the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A woman divorced irrevocably by a terminally ill husband observes the longer of the two iddahs: iddat al-talaq or iddat al-wafat.

This lesson addresses a unique Hanafi ruling: when a terminally ill husband divorces his wife irrevocably and then dies during her iddah, which waiting period does she observe? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement between the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and Abu Yusuf. The ruling is built on the principle that the marriage is considered extant for inheritance, and by extension, for iddah as a precaution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The scenario involves a husband in marad al-mawt (terminal illness) who divorces his wife irrevocably (talaq ba'in) and then dies while she is still in her iddah. The question is: which iddah must she complete?
This is separate from the ordinary case where a husband divorces while healthy.
2
State the majority Hanafi position
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the wife observes the longer of the two terms: iddat al-talaq (3 quru') or iddat al-wafat (4 months 10 days).
This is called 'ab'adu al-ajalayn' and is applied as a precaution (ihtiyat).
3
State the opposing view and its proof
Abu Yusuf holds that she only observes iddat al-talaq because the marriage ended by divorce before death, so the iddah of death is not triggered.
Abu Yusuf's dalil: the marriage is severed by talaq, so iddat al-wafat would only apply if death occurred while married.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The majority argue that since the marriage is considered extant for inheritance (the wife inherits despite the divorce if he dies in iddah), it should similarly be considered extant for iddah as a precaution.
This is an application of qiyas based on the inherited link (irth).
5
Determine the practical consequence
Under the majority view, the wife may end up observing up to four months and ten days even if her iddat al-talaq is shorter. Under Abu Yusuf, she only completes the shorter iddah.
The mufta bihi is the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولامرأة الفار: للبائن أبعد الأجلين: أي إن انقضَتْ عدة الطلاق، وهي ثلاث حيض مثلاً، ولم تنقض عدَّةُ الموت، فلا بُدَّ أن تتربص انقضاء عدة الموت، ولو انقضت عدَّةُ الموت، ولم تنقض عدَّةُ الطلاق، فلا بُدَّ أن تتربَّص عدَّةُ الطلاق. حاصله أنّ المريض مرض الموت إذا طلق امرأته ثلاثاً أو واحدة بائنة، ثم مات وهي في العدة تعتد احتياطاً أبعد الأجلين؛ أي أجل الطلاق وهو ثلاثة قروء، وأجلُ الموتِ وهو أربعة أشهر وعشر، وهذا عندهما. وعند أبي يوسف له تعتد عدة الطلاق؛ لأنّ النكاح قد انقطع بالطلاق قبل الموت، ولزمتها ثلاث حيض، وإنّما تجب عدة الوفاة إذا زال النكاح بالموت. ولهما: إنّ النكاح الفار باق في حق الإرث، حتى ترك امرأة الفار إذا مات كما مر، فيجعل باقياً في حق العدة احتياطاً.
And for a woman of al-farr: for the irrevocably divorced woman, the longer of the two terms: i.e., if the iddah of divorce is completed, which is three menstruations for example, and the iddah of death has not been completed, then she must wait for the completion of the iddah of death. And if the iddah of death is completed and the iddah of divorce has not been completed, then she must wait for the iddah of divorce. Its summary: The person who is terminally ill, if he divorces his wife three times or one irrevocable divorce, then dies while she is in iddah, she observes caution by taking the longer of the two terms: i.e., the term of divorce which is three quru', and the term of death which is four months and ten days. This is according to them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). According to Abu Yusuf: she observes the iddah of divorce, because the marriage was severed by divorce before death, and three menstruations became obligatory upon her; the iddah of death is only obligatory if the marriage ends by death. And according to them: the marriage of al-farr remains in effect regarding inheritance, as the ruling of the woman of al-farr when he dies, as previously mentioned, so it is considered remaining in effect regarding iddah as a precaution.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the iddah of a woman who is divorced by her husband while he is terminally ill (marad al-mawt). The relied-upon position (according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the two companions) is that she must observe the longer of the two waiting periods: either the iddah of divorce (three menstrual cycles) or the iddah of death (four months and ten days), whichever expires later. Abu Yusuf holds that she only observes the iddah of divorce, because the marriage ended by divorce before death, so the iddah of death is not applicable. The majority argue that since the marriage is still considered extant for inheritance purposes (to prevent the wife from being deprived of inheritance), it should similarly be considered extant for iddah as a precaution. This ruling is an application of the principle of precaution (ihtiyat) in ambiguous situations.

Key Terms
الفار (al-farr): A husband who divorces his wife while terminally ill (marad al-mawt) with the intention of depriving her of inheritance.
أبعد الأجلين (ab'adu al-ajalayn): The longer of the two waiting periods: iddat al-talaq (3 quru') and iddat al-wafat (4 months 10 days).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires a woman divorced irrevocably by a terminally ill husband to observe the longer of the two iddahs as a precautionary measure to protect her inheritance rights.

No Lineage from a Minor Husband
Ruling that a pregnancy cannot be attributed to a minor husband

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on lineage from a minor husband and apply the qiyas-based illa of possibility of intercourse.

Core Idea
A minor cannot father a child; lineage requires possibility of conception.

The Hanafi school holds that a pregnancy cannot be attributed to a husband who was a minor at the time of marriage, because conception is biologically impossible. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, responds to an objection based on the hadith 'al-walad lil-firash', and clarifies the role of marriage in establishing lineage.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify the ruling: lineage of a child from a minor husband is not established, whether the pregnancy began before his death or after it.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the primary dalil
The ruling is based on the impossibility of conception: a minor has no seminal fluid (mani), so intercourse cannot lead to pregnancy.
Qiyas with a case where intercourse is impossible.
3
Analyze the objection
An objector argues that marriage itself should establish lineage, citing the hadith 'al-walad lil-firash' and the principle of caution (ihtiyat) used in other lineage cases.
The hadith is general, and caution is usually applied in favor of lineage.
4
Understand the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response restricts the substitution of marriage for actual intercourse to cases where intercourse is physically possible. Since a minor cannot engage in intercourse, the general rule does not apply.
The illa (effective cause) is the possibility of intercourse, not marriage per se.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference between Hanafi and Shafi'i positions: for a minor husband, Hanafis deny lineage, while Shafi'is establish it based on marriage alone.
This affects inheritance, custody, and maintenance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And there is no lineage in its two aspects, meaning: in the case of pregnancy before the death of the boy or after it. And for the woman of the terminally ill: for the final divorce the longer of the two terms, meaning: if the iddah of divorce expires, which is three menstruations for example, but the iddah of death has not expired, then she must wait for the expiration of the death iddah; and if the death iddah expires but the divorce iddah has not expired, then she must wait for the divorce iddah due to pregnancy, because it is certain that its existence (the pregnancy) occurred at that time. This is in al-'Inayah. [1] His statement: 'and no lineage' means that the lineage of that pregnancy from the deceased minor husband is not established in the two scenarios: i.e., when the pregnancy existed at the time of the minor's death, and when it occurred after his death, because the minor has no seminal fluid, so conception from him is inconceivable, and lineage is a branch of its possibility. If you say: marriage exists, so it takes the place of fluid, and lineage is established due to the hadith: 'The child belongs to the bed' – narrated by al-Bukhari and others – and they have been cautious in establishing lineage to the extent that they said: if a man from the East marries a woman from the West and she gives birth after six months or more from the time of marriage, his lineage is established from him; so caution should be applied here as well. I say: caution is required and marriage takes the place of fluid only in a place where intercourse is conceivable, but our case is not like that.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage deals with two distinct rulings. The first ruling concerns the lineage of a child born to a widow whose deceased husband was a minor (a boy who has not reached puberty). The commentator clarifies that regardless of whether the pregnancy existed before the minor's death or after it, the lineage from the minor husband is not established. The reason is that a minor cannot produce semen, so conception is impossible. Lineage is conditional upon the possibility of conception. The text then raises an objection: if marriage itself can substitute for actual intercourse in establishing lineage (as per the hadith 'The child belongs to the bed,' i.e., the married couple is presumed to have intercourse), then why not here? The answer is that the substitution of marriage for intercourse only applies when intercourse is physically possible. In the case of a minor husband, intercourse is impossible, so the rule does not apply. The second ruling is for a woman whose husband divorces her with a final divorce (bain) while terminally ill, and then dies. She must observe the longer of the two waiting periods: the iddah of divorce (three menstrual cycles) or the iddah of mourning (four months and ten days). The reason given is caution (ihtiyat), because both obligations are possible. This is stated according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The commentary here is from 'al-'Inayah' and 'al-Hidayah'.

Key Terms
نسب (nasab): Lineage or paternity of a child from the husband
فراش (firāsh): The marital bed, a metaphor for marriage

Key Takeaway
Lineage from a minor husband is not established because conception is biologically impossible; marriage only substitutes for intercourse when intercourse is conceivable.

Longer of Two Iddahs for the Terminally Ill Divorcing Husband
Ruling and evidence for the longer iddah in terminal illness divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the iddah of a woman divorced with a final divorce by a terminally ill husband who dies during her iddah, and explain the evidence and the intra-madhhab dispute.

Core Idea
A terminally ill husband's divorce does not sever inheritance; thus, caution requires the longer iddah.

This lesson addresses a critical overlap between divorce and death iddahs. When a terminally ill husband divorces his wife with a final divorce and then dies while she is still in her divorce iddah, must she observe the longer of the two iddahs or only the divorce iddah? The Hanafi school answers based on the principle that the marriage is still considered intact for inheritance purposes, which extends to iddah as a precaution. We will walk through the evidence and the differing opinions within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the contested ruling
The case: a terminally ill husband divorces his wife with a final (ba'in) divorce and then dies while she is in her iddah. The question is which iddah she must observe: the divorce iddah (three menstrual cycles) or the longer of the two (divorce iddah and death iddah of four months and ten days).
The term 'al-farr' (fleeing) refers to a husband who divorces to deprive his wife of inheritance.
2
State the ruling attributed to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) in the text
According to the text, the two companions (see note about possible scribal error) hold that she must observe the longer of the two iddahs as a precaution (ihtiyat).
Standard Hanafi sources attribute the longer iddah to Abu Hanifa and the shorter to the two. This text has a reversed attribution. The relied-upon position is the longer iddah.
3
Analyse the opposing argument (attributed to Abu Yusuf in the text)
The opposing view argues that the marriage was severed by the divorce before death, so only the divorce iddah (three menstrual periods) applies. The death iddah only applies when the marriage ends by death.
The text explicitly says 'ind Abi Yusuf', but this may be a scribal error; standard sources attribute this view to the two companions.
4
Examine the evidence for the precautionary view
The two companions (as per the text) argue that the marriage of the 'fleeing' husband remains intact for the purpose of inheritance (the wife inherits despite the divorce). By analogy, it should also remain intact for iddah as a precaution, since the existence of pregnancy is uncertain and caution demands the longer period.
This is a qiyas based on the continued effect of the marriage in inheritance law.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position
Despite the attribution confusion, the later Hanafi authorities accept the longer iddah as the mufta bihi because it is the cautious and more comprehensive position.
The practical consequence: the woman must wait the longer of the two iddahs to be certain her iddah is complete.

No, the text says 'وهذا عندهما' meaning according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And then it says 'وعند أبي يوسف له تعتد عدة الطلاق' – that seems contradictory. Possibly the text is referring to a different opinion. I'll follow the literal: 'And according to Abu Yusuf, she observes the iddah of divorce'? But then 'لهما' is used for the two. The translation: 'And according to Abu Yusuf, she observes the iddah of divorce; because the marriage was severed by divorce before death, and three menstruations became obligatory upon her, and the death iddah is only required when the marriage ends by death. And according to the two: the marriage of the fleeing (terminally ill) remains with respect to inheritance, as the ruling of the woman of the fleeing when he dies as mentioned before, so it is considered remaining with respect to iddah as a precaution. Thus in al-Hidayah.'
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage addresses the case of a woman whose husband divorces her with a final divorce (bain) while he is terminally ill, and then dies during her iddah. The commentators present two views. The first view, attributed to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), is that she must observe the longer of the two iddahs: three menstrual cycles for the divorce, and four months and ten days for the death. The reason is precaution (ihtiyat): since the existence of the pregnancy is uncertain, both obligations are considered, and the longer period ensures fulfillment. The second view is attributed to Abu Hanifa (or possibly Abu Yusuf? The text says 'عند أبي يوسف' but this is unusual; the standard Hanafi position is that Abu Hanifa held the longer iddah view, while the two companions held the divorce iddah only. However, the text here says the longer iddah is 'cindahuma' (according to the two) and then states 'cinda Abi Yusuf' the opposite. This may be a confusion in the text. According to reliable sources (Hidayah, etc.), the longer iddah is the view of Imam Abu Hanifa, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that she only observes the iddah of divorce. The passage likely contains a scribal error. The argument for the longer iddah is that the marriage is still considered intact for the purpose of inheritance (the wife inherits from the terminally ill husband despite the divorce), so it should also be considered intact for iddah. The opposing view argues that the marriage was severed by the divorce, so only the divorce iddah applies. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the longer iddah according to caution.

Key Terms
الفار (al-fārr): Term for a terminally ill husband who divorces his wife
أبعد الأجلين (ab‛ad al-ajalayn): The longer of the two waiting periods: divorce iddah (3 quru') and death iddah (4 months 10 days)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
