A) Only three menstrual cycles (iddah of divorce)
B) Only four months and ten days (iddah of death)
C) The longer of the two iddahs as a precaution
D) She must complete both iddahs consecutively

Key Takeaway
A woman divorced with a final divorce by a terminally ill husband must observe the longer of the divorce iddah and the death iddah as a precaution.

Revocable Divorce: Death Transforms Iddah
Ruling that a revocably divorced woman upon husband's death shifts to death iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a revocably divorced woman's husband dies before her iddah ends, and explain the Hanafi reasoning based on the continuation of the marriage.

Core Idea
A revocable divorce does not sever the marriage; death of the husband triggers the death iddah, replacing the divorce iddah.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario in family law: a woman who has been given a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) and is still in her iddah when her ex-husband dies. According to the Hanafi school, she must immediately switch to the iddah of death (four months and ten days). This ruling is agreed upon by the three senior imams of the school, and its basis is that a revocable divorce does not completely dissolve the marriage tie.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the act
The scenario: a terminally ill husband divorces his wife revocably, and dies before her iddah of divorce (three menstrual cycles) is complete. The act in question is the iddah she must observe after his death.
The key is that the divorce is revocable (raj'i), not irrevocable (ba'in).
2
State the ruling and its hukm level
The ruling is that the death iddah (four months and ten days) becomes obligatory (wajib) upon her, replacing the remaining divorce iddah. This is a consensus among the Hanafi imams.
The ruling is not simply a continuation; it is a complete substitution.
3
Explain the legal reason (illah)
The marriage is not completely severed by a revocable divorce; the husband can take her back during the iddah without a new contract. Therefore, at the moment of his death, the marital relationship is considered intact, making her a widow subject to death iddah.
This reasoning is based on the principle that 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' – the marriage tie is effectively still present.
4
Confirm the intra-madhhab consensus
This ruling is agreed upon by Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the three imams of the school). There is no disagreement within the Hanafi school on this point.
The source text explicitly states 'bi-ittifaq a'immatina' (by agreement of our imams).

Source Text

Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage states a ruling that is agreed upon by all Hanafi imams. If a woman is divorced with a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) and her husband dies before her iddah is complete, she must observe the iddah of death (four months and ten days) instead of the remaining iddah of divorce. The reason is clear: a revocable divorce does not end the marriage completely; the husband can take her back during the iddah without a new contract. Therefore, at the moment of his death, the marriage is still intact, and the wife is considered a widow, subject to the death iddah and inheritance.

Emancipation During Iddah: Revocable vs Final
Ruling on change of iddah upon emancipation depending on divorce type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iddah transformation when a slave woman is emancipated, and apply it correctly to cases of revocable divorce, final divorce, and death.

Core Idea
Emancipation during revocable iddah upgrades the waiting period; during final iddah it does not — the marriage tie matters.

In Hanafi fiqh, the iddah (waiting period) of a slave woman is shorter than that of a free woman. But what happens if she is freed while in the middle of her iddah? The answer depends on the type of divorce. This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah clarifies the rule, explaining that the ongoing existence of the marriage in a revocable divorce allows the change, while a final divorce or death severs the marriage, so the emancipation has no effect on the iddah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the woman's status and divorce type
Determine if the woman is a slave or free, and if the divorce is revocable (raj‘i) or final (ba’in), or if it is death of the husband.
The ruling only applies to a married slave woman whose husband has divorced her or died.
2
Determine the initial iddah length
For a slave woman, the iddah is two menstrual cycles or two months; for a free woman, it is three cycles or three months.
The initial iddah is based on her status at the time of divorce or death.
3
Check timing of emancipation relative to iddah
Emancipation must occur during the iddah period. If it occurs before the divorce or after the iddah has ended, different rules apply.
If before divorce, she would have started a free woman’s iddah. If after iddah, she observes a new iddah for the master.
4
Apply the transformation rule
If the divorce is revocable and emancipation occurs during iddah, her iddah transforms to that of a free woman. If the divorce is final or death, it does not transform; she completes the slave iddah.
The reason is that the marriage bond remains intact in revocable divorce, so it is as if she became free while still married.
5
Calculate the remaining iddah
She does not start the iddah anew; she counts what she has already observed and completes the remainder according to the new length.
For example, if she observed one cycle as a slave and was freed during revocable iddah, she needs two more cycles (total three).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ولمن أُعتقت في عدة رجعي كعدة حرّة ) : أي عدتها كعدة حرة، (وفي عدة بائن، أو موت كأمة) : أي عدتها كعدة أمة.
[2] قوله : ولمن أعتقت ... الخ ؛ يعني أن الأمة ذات الزوج إذا طلقها زوجها طلاقاً رجعيّاً واعتدت ولم تنقض عدّتها، وهي حيضتان حتى أعتقها مولاها وصارت حرة ، فحينئذ تنتقل عدتها إلى عدة الحرائر، وهي ثلاث حيض لمن تحيض، وثلاثة أشهر لمن لا تحيض؛ لأنّ النكاح يبقى في الرجعي، فصار كأنها صارت حرّة حال قيام النكاح. بخلاف ما إذا طلقها الزوج بائناً أو مات، ثمّ أعتقها المولى في عدتها، فإنّه حينئذ لا تنتقل عدتها إلى عدة الحرائر ؛ لانقطاع النكاح عند العتق. [٣] قوله : في عدة رجعي ؛ أفاد به أنَّ العتق بعد طلاق الزوج في العدة؛ إذ لو كان قبله لزمتها عدة الحرّة ابتداء ، ولا تجب عليها عدة العتق، فإن عدة العتق لا تجب في منكوحة الغير، ولو كان العتق بعد انقضاء عدتها أو مات المولى بعد انقضاء عدتها من طلاق زوجها لزمها التربص ثلاث حيض، وهو عدة عتق المولى أو موته. [0] قوله : كأمة ؛ أي إذا طلقها الزوج طلاقاً بائناً أو مات زوجها فاعتدت ولم تنقض العدة، وهي حيضتان أو شهران وخمسة أيام . أعتقها المولى لم تنتقل عدتها
And for one who is emancipated during a revocable iddah, it is like the iddah of a free woman; and during a final or death iddah, it is like that of a slave woman. [2] His statement: 'and for one who is emancipated...' means: a married slave woman, if her husband divorces her with a revocable divorce and she enters iddah, and before her iddah expires (e.g., after two menstruations) her master emancipates her and she becomes free, then her iddah transforms to the iddah of free women: three menstruations for those who menstruate, and three months for those who do not. This is because the marriage remains in a revocable divorce, so it is as if she became free while the marriage existed. Contrary to if the husband divorces her with a final divorce or dies, and then her master emancipates her during her iddah: then her iddah does not transform to that of free women, because the marriage is severed at the time of emancipation. [3] His statement: 'during a revocable iddah' indicates that the emancipation occurs after the husband's divorce during the iddah; if it were before the divorce, she would have begun with a free woman's iddah, and the iddah of emancipation would not be obligatory, for the iddah of emancipation is not obligatory for one married to another. And if the emancipation occurred after her iddah had expired, or if the master died after the expiration of her iddah from her husband's divorce, she would have to wait three menstruations, which is the iddah of the master's emancipation or death. [4] His statement: 'like the iddah of a free woman' means three menstruations or three months, and she does not have to start the iddah anew; rather she counts what has passed and completes the remainder. [0] His statement: 'like a slave woman' means: if the husband divorces her with a final divorce or dies, and she enters iddah but has not completed it, e.g., two menstruations or two months and five days, then her master emancipates her, her iddah does not transform.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage explains the effect of emancipation on the length of iddah for a slave woman. The ruling distinguishes between revocable and final divorce or death. If the husband divorces her revocably and she is still in the iddah when freed, her iddah changes to that of a free woman (three menstrual cycles or three months for those who do not menstruate). The reason is that the marriage still exists in a revocable divorce, so it is as if she became free while still married. However, if the divorce is final (bain) or the husband dies, the marriage is already severed, so emancipation during the iddah has no effect; she remains with the slave iddah (two periods or two months and five days). The commentary also clarifies that she does not start the iddah anew but completes the remaining period based on the new length. For example, if she had already observed one menstruation as a slave, she needs two more as a free woman (total three). Additionally, the passage notes that if emancipation occurs before the divorce, she would have started with a free woman's iddah from the beginning. Also, if the emancipation or master's death occurs after her iddah from the husband is complete, she must observe a separate iddah for the master (three periods) because the marriage to the husband has ended.

Key Terms
عدة (ʿiddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband
طلاق رجعي (talāq raj‘ī): Revocable divorce – a divorce that allows the husband to take his wife back without a new marriage contract during the iddah.
طلاق بائن (talāq bā’in): Final divorce – irrevocable divorce that severs the marriage immediately.

Key Takeaway
The effect of emancipation on iddah depends on whether the marriage is still intact: revocable divorce allows transformation to free woman's iddah; final divorce or death does not.

Transition from Divorce 'Iddah to Death 'Iddah for a Revocably Divorced Woman
Ruling on transition from divorce 'iddah to death 'iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a revocably divorced woman's husband dies during her 'iddah and explain that the basis is ijma of the Hanafi imams due to the persistence of the marital tie.

Core Idea
Marriage endures in legal effect during revocable divorce; death during 'iddah triggers death 'iddah, not divorce 'iddah.

This lesson examines a key intersection of divorce and inheritance law: when a revocably divorced woman's husband dies before her 'iddah expires, the Hanafi school imposes the death 'iddah. The commentator draws on the principle that revocable divorce does not fully sever the marital bond, and the ruling is established by consensus of the Hanafi imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize the specific situation: a man in death-illness divorces his wife revocably and dies before the expiration of her divorce 'iddah (three menstrual cycles).
The scenario is used to illustrate the general rule.
2
Determine the ruling
State the ruling: the woman must observe 'iddat al-wafat (four months and ten days) instead of completing the divorce 'iddah. This 'iddah is wajib.
The ruling applies regardless of whether the husband was ill, as long as the divorce is revocable.
3
Note the proof
The proof is ijma (consensus) of the Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar). This is a strong dalil within the madhhab.
Ijma is considered qat'i in thubut but zanni in dalala for non-mujtahids.
4
Understand the rationale
The marriage is not completely severed by a revocable divorce; it remains intact for legal effects such as inheritance and 'iddah. Therefore, at the husband's death, the woman is still a wife and must observe death 'iddah.
This ta'lil (ratio legis) is key to distinguishing from irrevocable divorce.

Source Text

The commentator explains that this applies when the husband divorces her in his terminal illness and dies before the expiration of three menstrual cycles. He notes that this is unanimous among the Hanafi imams, because revocable divorce does not completely terminate the marriage; the woman is still considered a wife at the time of death, hence she inherits and must observe death 'iddah.

Key Terms

Effect of Emancipation on a Slave Woman's 'Iddah
Ruling on a slave woman's 'iddah when freed during divorce or death 'iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a slave woman's 'iddah changes upon emancipation and explain the marital status condition that determines the outcome.

Core Idea
Emancipation upgrades 'iddah only if the marriage is still intact (revocable divorce), not if already severed.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains a key distinction in Hanafi fiqh: a slave woman's emancipation during her waiting period does not always change her 'iddah. The deciding factor is whether the marriage persists at the time of emancipation. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the legal effect of revocable versus irrevocable divorce on marital continuity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage contrasts revocable divorce (raj'ī) with irrevocable divorce (bā'in) or death of the husband.
2
Determine marriage status at emancipation
In raj'ī, the marriage contract remains valid during 'iddah; in bā'in or death, it is already terminated.
3
Apply the ruling
If marriage intact, emancipation upgrades 'iddah to free woman's (three cycles/months); if not, she continues as a slave (two cycles/45 days).
4
Understand the 'illa
The change is because a free woman's 'iddah applies when the marriage is ongoing; once severed, status at emancipation is fixed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولمن أعتقت في عدة رجعي كعدة حرّة، وفي عدة بائن، أو موت كأمة. يعني أن الأمة ذات الزوج إذا طلقها زوجها طلاقاً رجعياً واعتدت ولم تنقض عدتها، وهي حيضتان حتى أعتقها مولاها وصارت حرة، فحينئذ تنتقل عدتها إلى عدة الحرائر، وهي ثلاث حيض لمن تحيض، وثلاثة أشهر لمن لا تحيض؛ لأن النكاح يبقى في الرجعي، فصار كأنها صارت حرّة حال قيام النكاح. بخلاف ما إذا طلقها الزوج بائناً أو مات، ثم أعتقها المولى في عدتها، فإنه حينئذ لا تنتقل عدتها إلى عدة الحرائر؛ لانقطاع النكاح عند العتق.
And for the one who is emancipated during a revocable 'iddah, her 'iddah is like that of a free woman; and during an irrevocable or death 'iddah, she is like a slave. Meaning: when a married slave woman is divorced by her husband revocably and she enters 'iddah, and before her 'iddah (two menstrual cycles) expires her master emancipates her, then her 'iddah changes to that of free women: three menstrual cycles for those who menstruate, three months for those who do not, because the marriage remains in revocable divorce, so it is as if she became free while the marriage was still intact. Unlike if the husband divorced her irrevocably or died, then the master emancipated her during her 'iddah, in which case her 'iddah does not change to that of free women, because the marriage is severed at the time of emancipation.

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on how a slave woman's 'iddah is affected if she is emancipated during her waiting period. The author explains that if the divorce was revocable (raj'ī), the marriage is still intact, so emancipation upgrades her 'iddah to that of a free woman. However, if the divorce was irrevocable (bā'in) or due to the husband's death, the marriage is already severed, and emancipation does not change her 'iddah; she completes it as a slave (two menstrual cycles or 45 days). The reasoning is that the 'iddah is tied to marital status: in revocable divorce, marriage persists, so she is considered a free wife upon emancipation.

Key Terms
البائن (al-bā'in): Irrevocable divorce: divorce that dissolves the marriage immediately, and the husband cannot take her back except with a new contract.
عدة الأمة ('iddat al-amah): The waiting period for a slave woman: two menstrual cycles or 45 days (for non-menstruating).

Substitute Lapses When Original Returns: Restarting 'Iddah After Menstruation
Ruling with proof and intra-madhhab ikhtilaf on blood definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a menopausal woman sees blood after completing her 'iddah by months, explain the legal principle that invalidates the substitute, and identify the mufta bihi definition of menstrual blood.

Core Idea
A substitute lapses when the original appears; menopausal blood restarts 'iddah as menstrual cycles.

This passage addresses a delicate situation in 'iddah law: a menopausal woman who has completed her three-month 'iddah then sees blood. Does she need to start over? The answer reveals a key legal principle: a substitute (months) is void when the original (menstruation) returns. Alongside the ruling, the Hanafi jurists debate what exactly counts as menstrual blood for a menopausal woman — flowing or scanty, red or yellow — and the fatwa settles on a specific criterion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
State the case: a menopausal woman (ā'isah) who observes 'iddah by three months then sees blood that indicates she is menstruating. The ruling: she must restart her 'iddah with three menstrual cycles.
The ruling applies regardless of how long after completing the months she sees the blood.
2
Understand the legal principle
The author justifies the ruling by a principle: 'iddah by months is a substitute (khalaf) for 'iddah by menstrual cycles. When the original (menstruation) appears, the substitute has no effect.
The Arabic phrase: 'la mu'tabara bi al-khalaf 'inda wujud al-asl'.
3
Examine the three interpretations of 'seeing blood'
Hanafi scholars differ on what qualifies as menstrual blood for a menopausal woman. The passage lists three opinions: (1) flowing profusely (sā'il kathīran), (2) red or black in color, (3) according to her previous habit.
These interpretations determine whether the trigger 'seeing blood' has occurred.
4
Identify the mufta bihi and the commentator's preference
The fatwa (operative position) is on the first opinion: flowing blood. The commentator notes his own earlier view from the chapter on menstruation uses a color criterion (red/black = hayd, yellow/green = istihadah).
The fatwa is practical: flux is easier to verify objectively than color or habit.

And a menopausal woman who sees blood after the 'iddah of months must restart with menstrual cycles. Meaning: if the wife is menopausal, her 'iddah is by months; she observes three months, then sees blood such that it becomes known she is menstruating, then she must observe 'iddah with three menstrual cycles, because 'iddah by months is a substitute for 'iddah by menstrual cycles, and the substitute is not considered when the original is present. And they differed on the meaning of 'seeing blood': some said it must be flowing profusely, excluding a little moisture; some said the blood must be red or black, not yellow, green, or muddy; some said it must be according to her habit. The fatwa is on the first. And the commentator's choice in the chapter of menstruation is that if she sees red or black after menopause, it is menstruation; if she sees green, muddy, or yellow, it is istihadah.

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where a menopausal woman (ā'isah) has completed her 'iddah of three months, but then sees blood that indicates she is still menstruating. According to Hanafi law, if such blood appears, her previous 'iddah by months is invalidated, and she must now observe 'iddah of three menstrual cycles. The logic is that months are a substitute (khalaf) for cycles, and the substitute becomes irrelevant when the original (menstruation) is present. The commentator also discusses the definition of 'seeing blood' for a menopausal woman: is it any blood, or must it meet certain criteria like being flowing, red/black, or in line with her previous habit? He notes the fatwa follows the first opinion (flowing profusely), but also mentions his own preference from the chapter on menstruation that color determines whether it is hayd or istihadah.

Key Terms
آيسة (ā'isah): A woman who has reached menopause, i.e., permanently ceased menstruation. Age disputed: 55 or 60.
عدة الأشهر ('iddat al-ashhur): The 'iddah by months: three months for a menopausal woman or a young girl who has not menstruated.

Menopausal Woman Seeing Blood During 'Iddah: Four Hanafi Views
Expansion of intra-Hanafi dispute on 'iddah restart

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four Hanafi views on whether a menopausal woman must restart her 'iddah when she sees blood after months and identify the practical consequence of each view.

Core Idea
When a substitute (months) is used, its validity depends on the permanence of the original's absence.

This lesson expands on a ruling from the matn: a menopausal woman who observes 'iddah by months and then sees blood. The Hanafi school records multiple positions on whether she must restart with menstruation. The disagreement revolves around the nature of the months as a substitute for menstruation and the definition of menopause itself. We will examine each view, its rationale, and its practical effect on the validity of a subsequent marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the woman and her 'iddah
The woman is ā'isah (menopausal). Her 'iddah is three months (al-‘iddatu bil-ashhur) because she does not menstruate. This is a substitute (khalaf) for the normal 'iddah of three menstruations.
Menopause is either determined by age (55 or 60) or by actual cessation of menstruation.
2
Understand the scenario
After beginning or completing her three-month 'iddah, she sees blood. The timing of this blood (before or after completion) and its characteristics (color, quantity, habit) affect the ruling.
The passage identifies multiple interpretations of 'sees blood': habit, color (red/black vs yellow/green), or quantity (flowing vs slight).
3
Learn the four Hanafi views
The text records four distinct positions on whether her 'iddah is broken and she must restart with menstruation: (1) no restart if blood appears after completion; (2) restart absolutely if blood appears according to habit; (3) never restart; (4) conditional on whether menopause has a fixed age.
Each view has a basis in Hanafi methodology: the substitute is valid only if the original is permanently absent.
4
Apply the practical consequence
If she remarried after completing three months but before seeing blood, under view (1) the marriage is valid; under view (2) it is invalid because the 'iddah was not over. This thamarah al-khilaf shows the real-world impact.
The text notes that al-Sadr al-Shahid gave fatwa on view (1).
5
Recognize the tarjih
The passage does not give a single tarjih but presents al-Sadr's view as 'the correct choice for fatwa' while acknowledging that al-Hidayah's view is also considered correct by many. The fourth view offers a compromise based on the two narrations on the age of menopause.
The student should note that intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is legitimate and the mufti may choose based on context and evidence.

Book of Divorce, Chapter of 'Iddah. And a menopausal woman who sees blood after (the period of) 'iddah by months, she begins anew with menstruation. And a menopausal woman who sees blood after (the period of) 'iddah by months, she begins anew with menstruation.) That is, if the wife is (observing the 'iddah) of a free woman, rather she must complete that 'iddah, and the 'iddah of emancipation is not obligatory upon her, for it is not obligatory for the 'iddah of another and for his married woman. (1) His saying: 'menopausal woman': she is the woman who has entered the age of menopause, which is cessation of menstruation, and they differed regarding its estimation. The commentator (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned here that it is fifty-five, and he attributed it in the chapter of menstruation to the scholars of Bukhara or Khwarazm, and said there: 'Most scholars estimated it as sixty years, and we have previously detailed it there, so remember it.' [2] His saying: 'sees blood'; the author of al-Hidayah said: 'Its meaning is when she sees blood according to habit.' End. And they differed regarding its meaning: It was said: its meaning is when it is flowing abundantly, as a precaution against when she sees a slight wetness. And it was said: its meaning is what was mentioned, and that the blood is red or black, not yellow, green, or muddy. And it was said: its meaning is that it be according to her usual habit, such that if her habit before menopause was yellow and she saw it accordingly, her menopause is overturned. And it was stated in al-Mi'raj that the fatwa is upon the first. Thus in al-Bahr and others. And the commentator (may Allah have mercy on him) has previously in the chapter of menstruation chosen that if she sees after menopause red or black, it is menstruation, and if she sees green, muddy, or yellow, it is istihadah. (3) His saying: 'after (the period of) 'iddah by months'; its apparent meaning is that she saw blood after completing her 'iddah by months, meaning her husband divorced her while she was menopausal, and her 'iddah is by months, so she observed 'iddah for three months, then she saw blood, such that it is known that she is among those who menstruate, then at that time it is obligatory upon her to observe 'iddah by three menstruations, because observing 'iddah by months is a substitute for observing 'iddah by menstruation, and the substitute is not considered when the original exists. On this basis, her marriage is invalidated if she married after completing the three months before seeing blood, due to its occurrence during the 'iddah. This is the apparent (meaning) of al-Hidayah, and the commentator interpreted the author's statement to mean after her observing 'iddah by months before completing the three months, and he clarified it in the chapter of menstruation. [From al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 4:151] Regarding the age of menopause: i.e., fifty-five years and above, and her blood has stopped, so her husband divorced her; she observes 'iddah by three months, and before their completion she sees blood, so it is known that she was not menopausal, so she begins anew with menstruation. He said in al-Hidayah: 'It is the correct (view).' And in the narration of Abu Ali al-Daqqaq: Whenever she sees blood after it has been ruled that she is menopausal, it is not menstruation, and menopause is not invalidated, and that does not appear in the invalidation of marriages, because it is blood that came out at other than its time. [1] His saying: 'before their completion'; he indicates that if she sees blood after completing the 'iddah by months, her 'iddah is not broken, and it is not obligatory upon her, and this is one of the six statements on this issue, and al-Sadr al-Shahid gave fatwa upon it, and he said in al-Mujtaba: 'It is the correct (view) chosen for fatwa.' The second: It is broken absolutely, and this is the apparent meaning of the statement of al-Hidayah where he said: 'If she was menopausal and observed 'iddah by months then saw blood, what has passed of her 'iddah is broken, and she must begin the 'iddah anew with menstruation.' Its meaning: when she sees blood according to habit, because her return (to menstruation) invalidates menopause—that is the correct (view). So it becomes clear that it was not a substitute, because the condition of substitutability is the realization of menopause, and that is by the continuation of inability until death, like the ransom for the decrepit old man. The third: It is not broken absolutely, and al-Ispijabi chose it. The fourth: It is broken according to the narration of not estimating menopause, which is the apparent narration, and it is not broken according to the narration of estimation, and al-Iydah chose it, and al-Bada'i' supported it.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) of a ruling from a matn concerning a menopausal woman who observes 'iddah by months and then sees blood. The commentator opens by clarifying that the woman must complete the 'iddah of a free woman, and there is no additional 'iddah for emancipation. He then explains the term 'menopausal woman' (A'isah) and notes the disagreement among scholars over the age of menopause: some say fifty-five, others sixty, the latter being the view of most scholars. Next, he expounds the meaning of 'sees blood' – citing al-Hidayah that it means seeing blood according to habit. There is further ikhtilaf on the description: some require flowing blood, others specify color (red/black not yellow/green), and others consider habit. The fatwa is given on the first opinion (flowing) according to al-Mi'raj. The commentator himself, in the chapter on menstruation, held that red or black is menstruation while other colors are istihadah. The core issue is whether seeing blood after having completed the 'iddah by months (i.e., after three months) or before completion triggers a restart of the 'iddah with menstruation. The apparent meaning of the text – and the view of al-Hidayah – is that restart is required only if the blood is seen before the completion of the three months, because the months are a substitute for menstruation; if the original capacity returns, the substitute is void. If she marries after three months but before the blood is seen, the marriage is invalid if blood appears later, indicating she was still in 'iddah. The commentator notes that al-Hidayah considers the correct view to be that if blood appears before completion, she was never truly menopausal and must start the 'iddah with menstruation. He then presents four distinct Hanafi positions on this issue: (1) If blood appears after completion, the 'iddah is not broken and no restart is required – this is the fatwa of al-Sadr al-Shahid and chosen in al-Mujtaba. (2) It is broken absolutely (even after completion) – this is the apparent view of al-Hidayah, supported by the argument that the reappearance of menstruation proves menopause was false, so the substitute never validly took effect. (3) It is not broken at all – chosen by al-Ispijabi. (4) It is broken if one follows the narration that menopause has no fixed age (the apparent narration), but not broken if one fixes an age (e.g., fifty-five) – chosen in al-Iydah and supported by al-Bada'i'. This exposition highlights the nuance within the Hanafi school: the ruling depends on whether the blood is seen before or after completion, and on the underlying principle of whether the months were a valid substitute.

Key Terms
آيسة (ā'isah): A woman who has reached the age of menopause (approximately 55 or 60) and whose menstruation has ceased completely.
العدة بالأشهر (al-'iddatu bil-ashhur): The waiting period calculated by months (three months) prescribed for a woman who does not menstruate due to menopause or young age.
الإياس (al-iyās): The state of menopause; permanent cessation of menstruation, either due to age or natural condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Her 'iddah is broken and she must restart with menstruation.
B) Her 'iddah is not broken and she does not need to restart.
C) It depends on whether the age of menopause is estimated.
D) She must restart only if the blood is yellow.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a menopausal woman's 'iddah by months is not necessarily invalidated by subsequent blood; there are four views, with the strongest being that if blood appears after completion, no restart is required.

When Menopausal Blood Returns: Six Hanafi Opinions on 'Iddah by Months
Exposition of six Hanafi opinions on seeing blood after menopause during 'iddah by months

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six Hanafi opinions on the effect of seeing blood after menopause during 'iddah by months and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Blood before completion disrupts; blood after completion does not—timing decides the 'iddah.

When a woman has reached the age of menopause (55+ years) and is divorced, she observes an 'iddah of three months instead of three menstrual cycles. But what happens if she unexpectedly sees blood before the three months are over? Does this mean she was never truly menopausal? Hanafi scholars have debated this extensively, giving rise to six distinct opinions. This lesson unpacks each view, the reasoning behind it, and the position relied upon for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base scenario and trigger event
A menopausal woman (55+ years, blood ceased) is divorced and begins 'iddah by three months. The trigger is seeing blood either before or after completing the three months.
The passage specifies 'before its completion' versus 'after completing.'
2
Understand the principle of substitution (khalafiyya)
The three months are a substitute for menstruation only if the woman is permanently incapable of menstruating. If she menstruates, the substitute is void.
Compare to ransom for an elderly man who cannot fast.
3
Examine the first opinion (mufta bihi)
If blood appears before the three months are complete, she restarts with menstruation. If after completion, no disruption. This is the fatwa position according to al-Sadr al-Shahid and al-Mujtaba.
This opinion is preferred for its consistency with the condition of substitution.
4
Compare the second and third opinions
Second opinion (from al-Hidayah): absolute disruption regardless of timing. Third opinion (al-Asbijabi): never disrupts. Understand the evidence: the second relies on the return of the menstrual habit nullifying menopause; the third considers post-menopausal blood as irregular (istihada).
Note the thamarah al-khilaf: practical consequences differ.
5
Analyze the remaining three opinions
Opinions four, five, and six add conditions: (4) depends on whether a time limit for menopause is set, (5) depends on a prior judicial ruling of menopause, (6) only disrupts for future divorces. Each has a basis in specific transmissions and principles.
These show the depth of Hanafi ikhtilaf.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في سن الإياس : أي خمسة وخمسين سنةً فصاعداً ، وقد انقطع دمها، فطلقها الزوج تعتد بثلاثة أشهر، فقبل انقضائها رأت الدم، فعلم أنها لم تكن آيسة فتستأنف بالحيض. قال في «الهداية»: هو الصحيح). وفي رواية أبي على الدقاق : إنها متى رأت الدم بعدما حكم بإياسها أنه لا يكون حيضاً، ولا يبطل الإياس، ولا يظهر ذلك في فساد الأنكحة ؛ لأنه دم خرج في غير أوانه [1] قوله : فقبل انقضائها ؛ يشير به إلى أنه لو رأت الدم بعد تمام العدة بالأشهر لا تنتقض عدتها، ولا يجب عليها، وهذا أحد الأقوال السنة في هذه المسألة، وأفتى به الصدر الشهيد، وقال في «المجتبى»: هو الصحيح المختار للفتوى. الثاني : إنه ينتقض مطلقاً، وهو ظاهر عبارة «الهداية» حيث قال : إن كانت آيسة فاعتدت بالشهور ثم رأت الدم انتقض ما مضى من عدتها، وعليها أن تستأنف العدة بالحيض، ومعناه : إذا رأت الدم على العادة ؛ لأن عودها يبطل الإياس هو الصحيح، فظهر أنه لم يكن خلفاً، وهذا لأن شرط الخلفية تحقق الإياس، وذلك باستدامة العجز إلى الممات، كالفدية في حق الشيخ الفاني. الثالث : إنه لا ينتقض مطلقاً، واختاره الاسبيجابي. الرابع : ينتقض على رواية عدم التقدير للإياس التي هي ظاهر الرواية، ولا ينتقض على رواية التقدير له، واختاره في «الإيضاح»، ونصره في «البدائع». الخامس : ينتقض إن لم يحكم بإياسها، وإن حكم به فلا، كأن يدعي أحدهما فساد النكاح فيقضي بصحته، وصححه في «الاختيار». السادس : ينتقض في المستقبل، فلا تعتد إلا بالحيض للطلاق بعده لا الماضي، وصححه في «النوازل». كذا في «رد المحتار».
Concerning the age of menopause: that is fifty-five years and above, and her blood has ceased. If her husband divorced her, she completes her waiting period of three months. If before its completion she sees blood, it becomes known that she was not menopausal, so she restarts [the waiting period] with menstruation. It is stated in al-Hidayah: 'It is the correct [view].' In the narration of Abu Ali al-Daqqaq: Whenever she sees blood after [a judge] has ruled her menopausal, it is not considered menstrual blood, it does not nullify the menopause, nor does it appear in the invalidity of marriages because it is blood that exited outside its [proper] time. [Footnote:] His statement: 'before its completion' indicates that if she saw blood after completing the waiting period by months, her waiting period is not disrupted and it is not obligatory upon her. This is one of the six opinions in this matter, and al-Sadr al-Shahid gave a fatwa accordingly. It is stated in al-Mujtaba: 'It is the correct choice for fatwa.' The second: It is disrupted absolutely, and this is the apparent wording of al-Hidayah where it says: If she was menopausal and completed the waiting period by months then saw blood, what has passed of her waiting period is disrupted and she must restart the waiting period with menstruation, meaning if she saw blood according to her usual habit, because her return [to menstruation] nullifies the menopause—this is the correct [view]. So it appears it was not a substitute, because the condition for being a substitute is the realization of menopause, which is the continuation of incapacity until death, like the ransom for the decrepit old man. The third: It is not disrupted absolutely, and al-Asbijabi chose it. The fourth: It is disrupted according to the transmission that does not set a time limit for menopause, which is the apparent transmission; and it is not disrupted according to the transmission that sets a time limit; al-Iydah chose this and it was championed in al-Bada'i. The fifth: It is disrupted if [the judge] has not ruled her menopausal; if he has ruled so, then no, such as when one of the two parties claims the marriage is invalid and he rules it valid. This was corrected in al-Ikhtiyar. The sixth: It is disrupted for the future; she only completes the waiting period by menstruation for the divorce after it, not the past. This was corrected in al-Nawazil. Thus quoted from Radd al-Muhtar.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of seeing blood after a woman has been deemed menopausal (aaisa) during her 'iddah by months. The base ruling (matn) states that if a woman in the age of menopause (55+ years) is divorced and begins her 'iddah by three months, but sees blood before completing it, she must restart with menstruation because it indicates she was never truly menopausal. Al-Hidayah confirms this as correct. However, a competing narration from Abu Ali al-Daqqaq holds that such blood is not menstrual and does not invalidate the menopause. The commentary then lists six distinct opinions. The first (the correct view for fatwa per al-Sadr al-Shahid and al-Mujtaba) distinguishes between seeing blood before vs. after the 'iddah is complete: before disrupts, after does not. The second (from al-Hidayah) says it disrupts absolutely because the return of menstruation proves the menopause was invalid. The third says it never disrupts. The fourth differentiates based on whether one adopts a time-limited definition of menopause. The fifth conditions disruption on the absence of a prior judicial ruling of menopause. The sixth disrupts only for future divorces. Each opinion has a basis in Hanafi principles of 'iddah, substitution (khalafiyya), and the nature of menopausal blood. The 'mufta bihi' position is the first opinion: only blood seen before completion of the months disrupts.

Key Terms
الإياس (al-iyās): The legal condition of a woman who has reached an age (55 years and above) where menstruation is no longer expected normally.
العدة بالأشهر (al-ʿiddah bil-ashhur): Waiting period of three months for a menopausal or prepubescent woman, as a substitute for three menstrual cycles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first opinion (the mufta bihi), what is the ruling if a menopausal woman sees blood after completing her three-month 'iddah?
A) Her 'iddah is disrupted and she must start over with menstruation.
B) Her 'iddah is not disrupted and no action is needed.
C) She must complete an additional three months.
D) She must repeat only the months after the blood.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi opinion differentiates based on timing: blood before completion invalidates the months; blood after completion does not.

Iddah for a Woman Who Menstruates Then Reaches Menopause
Ruling and critique on 'iddah for a menopausal woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a woman who reaches menopause after menstruating once or twice following divorce, and explain why she must restart a full three-month 'iddah.

Core Idea
Menstruations and months cannot overlap—she must start a fresh three-month 'iddah from menopause.

This lesson examines the 'iddah of a woman who begins to menstruate after divorce but reaches menopause before completing three cycles. The Hanafi school holds that she must begin a fresh three-month period, disregarding time already passed. The reasoning draws on the principle that a substitute and its original cannot coexist. We will unpack the ruling and the objection raised against it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A woman is divorced while menstruating, menstruates once or twice, then reaches menopause before completing three cycles. Her blood ceases due to age.
The key is that menopause occurs mid-'iddah, changing the type of 'iddah required.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
She must complete 'iddah by three months, counting from the time of menopause, not from the divorce. The past time is irrelevant.
This is to avoid combining the substitute (months) with the original (menstruations).
3
Understand the justification
Three months are a substitute (badal) for three menstrual cycles (mubdal). If she counted past months, she would be using both simultaneously, which is prohibited by qiyas with tayammum and wudu.
The principle: 'jam' bayn al-badal wa al-mubdal' is invalid.
4
Consider the objection
An objector argues that 'iddah obligation begins at divorce, and at that time she was menstruating. Therefore, her 'iddah should be by menstruations, not months. The shift to months seems arbitrary.
The text cuts off before the response, but the Hanafi response would be that the type of 'iddah is determined by the condition at the end of the period, not the beginning.
5
Apply the ruling
Practice: a woman divorced, menstruates twice, then reaches menopause. She must observe three months, not one. If she menstruates once, she must observe three months, not two.
No partial credit for past menstruations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(كما تستأنف بالشهور من حاضَتْ حيضة ثم أيست) : أي انقطع دمها ، وهي في سن الإياس تستأنف بالشهور [1] قوله : حيضة ; وكذا حيضتين، والحاصل أنه طلقها وهي من ذوات الحيض فحاضت بعد الطلاق حيضة أو حيضتين، ثم دخلت في سن اليأس وانقطع دمها ، فحينئذ يجب عليها أن تعتد بالشهور تحرزا عن الجمع بين البدل والمبدل، كذا علله في «الهداية». وظاهره أنه لا عبرة بما مضى فلا يعتبر مع الحيضة وقت حيضتين – أي شهرين - إن حاضت حيضة واحدة ثم أيست ، ولا يعتبر مع الحيضتين وقت حيضة ؛ أي شهر بعده ، بل يجب عليها الاعتداد بأشهر ثلاثة مستقلة ؛ لئلا يلزم الجمع بين البدل والمبدل، فإن ثلاثة أشهر بدل من ثلاث حيض، هذا ما ذكره الشارح الهروي ، ودفع به الإشكال الذي أورده الشارح . [2] قوله : أقول...الخ ; إيراد على ما حكم به المصنف له بالاستئناف، وذلك بوجهين : أحدهما : إن وجوب العدة يعقب الطلاق، وتجب متصلة به، والمفروض أنها عند الطلاق كانت من ذوات الحيض، حتى حاضت حيضة أو حيضتين، ثم أيست بعده، فلا يظهر كون عدتها بالأشهر من وقت الطلاق، حتى تجب ثلاثة أشهر ؛ لكون الإياس متأخرا عن وقت الطلاق، بخلاف ما تقدم، فإن رؤية الدم هناك بعد اليأس تحكم بأنها لم تكن آيسة من وقت الطلاق، بل كانت من ذوات الحيض الممتد طهرها
(Similarly, she restarts [the waiting period] with months if she menstruated once then became menopausal): meaning her blood ceased, and she is in the age of menopause, so she restarts with months. [Footnote:] His statement: 'one menstruation'; and likewise two menstruations. The summary: He divorced her while she was among those who menstruate, she menstruated after the divorce once or twice, then entered the age of menopause and her blood ceased. At that point, she must complete her waiting period by months, to avoid combining the substitute and the substituted. This is how al-Hidayah explains it. Apparent from it is that no consideration is given to what has passed; thus one does not count with one menstruation the time of two menstruations—i.e., two months—if she menstruated once then became menopausal; nor with two menstruations the time of one menstruation—i.e., a month after it—but she must complete three independent months, to avoid combining the substitute and the substituted. For three months are a substitute for three menstruations. This is what the commentator al-Harawi mentioned, and with it he repelled the objection raised by the commentator. [Footnote:] His statement: 'I say...etc.' is an objection to what the author ruled regarding her restarting, and that in two ways: One: The obligation of 'iddah follows the divorce and must be connected to it, and the assumption is that at the time of divorce she was among those who menstruate, until she menstruated once or twice, then she became menopausal after that. So it is not apparent that her 'iddah by months starts from the time of divorce, such that three months become obligatory, because the menopause occurs after the time of divorce. Unlike what preceded, for the sighting of blood there after menopause ruled that she was not menopausal at the time of divorce, but rather was among those who menstruate with an extended clean period.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling for a woman who is divorced while still menstruating, menstruates once or twice, and then reaches menopause before completing her 'iddah by menstruation. According to al-Hidayah, she must restart a full three-month 'iddah by months, disregarding the time already passed since the divorce. The reason is to avoid combining the substitute (months) with the substituted (menstruations) in the same period. If she had one menstruation, she does not count the two months that would correspond to two more menstruations; if she had two, she does not count the one month for the third. Instead, she must observe three whole months. The commentator al-Harawi explains this as a way to prevent the forbidden combination of the original and its replacement. The passage then records an objection (irad) by the commentator: since the 'iddah obligation immediately follows the divorce, and at that time she was menstruating, it is unclear how her 'iddah can be by months starting from the divorce. The menopause occurred later, so the months 'iddah should only begin from when she becomes menopausal, not from the divorce. This objection is based on the principle that the type of 'iddah is determined at the time of divorce. The text cuts off, likely continuing with a response.

Key Terms
البدل والمبدل (al-badal wa al-mubdal): The substitute (months) and the thing substituted (menstruations). The rule is they cannot be combined in a single period.
العِدّة (al-'iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death before remarriage.
الإياس (al-i'yas): The age at which a woman ceases to menstruate permanently, typically around 55 years in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman is divorced, menstruates once, then reaches menopause. How must she complete her 'iddah?
A) She completes the remaining two months counting from the divorce.
B) She observes one more month, totaling three months with the one menstruation counting as two months.
C) She observes three complete months from the time of menopause, ignoring the past.
D) She waits until she menstruates again, even if it takes years.

Key Takeaway
A woman who reaches menopause after menstruating post-divorce must restart a full three-month 'iddah, because the substitute (months) cannot overlap with the original (menstruations).

Iddah of a Woman Becoming Menopausal After Menstruating
Explains the iddah ruling when menstruation occurs then menopause sets in

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a woman who menstruates after divorce and then reaches menopause, and explain why she must restart her iddah with three months.

Core Idea
Once menopausal during iddah, she must restart with three months — the substitute cannot combine with the original.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, the Hanafi school addresses a specific iddah scenario: a woman menstruates after divorce, then becomes menopausal. The ruling may seem counterintuitive — she must discard the time that has already passed and begin a fresh three-month iddah. The key is the fiqh principle of avoiding combining a substitute (badal) with the original (mubdal).

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the explicit ruling given in the matn: the woman resumes her iddah with three months from the time of menopause, regardless of whether she had one or two menstruations.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited: the principle of avoiding combining the badal (months) and the mubdal (menstrual cycles), mentioned in al-Hidayah.
The Arabic phrase: تحرزاً عن الجمع بين البدل والمبدل
3
Understand the objection
The commentator raises two objections: first, the iddah obligation begins at divorce, so elapsed time should count; second, if iddah is by months from divorce, the previous time should still count as time passing.
The objections are based on the immediacy of the iddah obligation.
4
Analyse the response
The Hanafi response: the resumption disregards the previous time only as a menstrual cycle (not absolutely), and the delayed menopause is considered from divorce to avoid combining the two bases.
The first objection is answered by considering the menopause from the time of divorce for the purpose of avoiding combination.
5
Consider the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: if the objection were accepted, the iddah would be shorter; with the Hanafi position, she must wait three full months after menopause.
This ensures no mixing of the two types of iddah.

كما تستأنف بالشهور من حاضت حيضة ثم أيست : أي انقطع دمها ، وهي في سن الإياس تستأنف بالشهور
أقول : الاستئناف مشكل ؛ لأنه لو ظهر أن عدتها بالأشهر من وقتِ الطلاق الخامس : ينتقض إن لم يحكم بإياسها ، وإن حكم به فلا ، كأن يدعي أحدهما فساد النكاح فيقضي بصحته، وصححه في «الاختيار». السادس : ينتقض في المستقبل، فلا تعتدّ إلا بالحيض للطلاق بعده لا الماضي، وصححه في «النوازل». كذا في «رد المحتار».
قوله : حيضة ؛ وكذا حيضتين، والحاصل أنه طلقها وهي من ذوات الحيض فحاضت بعد الطلاق حيضة أو حيضتين، ثم دخلت في سن اليأس وانقطع دمها ، فحينئذ يجب عليها أن تعتدّ بالشهور تحرزاً عن الجمع بين البدل والمبدل، كذا علّله في «الهداية». وظاهره أنه لا عبرة بما مضى فلا يعتبر مع الحيضة وقت حيضتين – أي شهرين - إن حاضت حيضةً واحدةً ثم أيست ، ولا يعتبر مع الحيضتين وقت حيضة ؛ أي شهر بعده ، بل يجب عليها الاعتداد بأشهر ثلاثة مستقلة ؛ لئلا يلزم الجمع بين البدل والمبدل، فإنّ ثلاثة أشهر بدل من ثلاث حيض، هذا ما ذكره الشارح الهروي ، ودفع به الإشكال الذي أورده الشارح .
قوله : أقول...الخ ؛ إيراد على ما حكم به المصنف له بالاستئناف، وذلك بوجهين : أحدهما : إنّ وجوب العدّة يعقب الطلاق، وتجب متصلة به، والمفروض أنها عند الطلاق كانت من ذوات الحيض، حتى حاضت حيضة أو حيضتين، ثم أيست بعده، فلا يظهر كون عدتها بالأشهر من وقت الطلاق، حتى تجب ثلاثة أشهر ؛ لكون الإياس متأخراً عن وقت الطلاق، بخلاف ما تقدم، فإنّ رؤية الدم هناك بعد اليأس تحكم بأنها لم تكن آيسة من وقت الطلاق، بل كانت من ذوات الحيض الممتد طهرها، فالحيض التي رأت قبل الإياس مشتملة على الوقت، فيجب أن يكون محسوباً من العدة من حيث أنه وقت.
وعلى معتدة وطئت بشبهة عدة أخرى، وتداخلتا فيلزم الاستئناف بالحيض، ويبطل ما مضى، وهاهنا لا يظهر بالإياس المتأخر أنها كانت آيسة من وقت الطلاق.
وثانيهما : أنه لو ظهر أنّ عدّتها بالأشهر من وقت الطلاق، فلا معنى للاستئناف أيضاً، بل يجب أن يكون زمان الحيضة أو الحيضتين الذي تقدم على الإياس محسوباً من العدة، من حيث أنه وقت لا من حيث أنه حيضة.
وأجيب عنه : بأن الاستئناف بعدم اعتبار الزمان السابق من حيث الحيضة، لا عدم اعتباره مطلقاً، وهذا وإن اندفع به الإيراد الثاني، لكن لا يندفع به الإيراد الأول، إلا أن يقال : إنما اعتبر الإياس المتأخر من وقتِ الطلاق تحرزاً عن الجمع بين البدل والمبدل منه.
قوله : على معتدة... الخ ؛ في إطلاق معتدة إشارة إلى تعميم الحكم في معتدة الطلاق، ومعتدة الوفاة، وفي إطلاق الوطء إشارة إلى تعميم الحكم في وطء الزوج المطلق، ووطء غيره، قال في «الدرر شرح الغرر»: المرأة إذا وجب عليها عدّتان ، فإمّا أن يكونا من رجلين أو من واحد، ففي الثاني لا شك أن العدتين تداخلتا.
ينظر : شرح ابن ملك»(ق١١٥/أ).
Book of Divorce, Chapter of 'Iddah. As she resumes with months when she menstruated once then became menopausal: i.e., her blood ceased and she is in the age of menopause, she resumes with months. I say: The resumption is problematic because if it appears that her 'iddah is by months from the time of divorce... fifth: it is invalidated if her menopause is not judged, and if judged then not, like if one of them claims the marriage is corrupt and the judge rules it valid, and it is corrected in 'al-Ikhtiyar'. Sixth: it is invalidated in the future, so she only observes 'iddah by menstruation for the divorce after it, not the past, and it is corrected in 'al-Nawazil'. Likewise in 'Radd al-Muhtar'. He said: 'one menstruation', likewise two menstruations, and the conclusion is that he divorced her while she was one who menstruates, then she menstruated after the divorce once or twice, then she entered the age of menopause and her blood stopped, so then it is obligatory upon her to observe 'iddah by months to avoid combining the substitute and the substituted, as it is reasoned in 'al-Hidayah'. Its apparent meaning is that no consideration is given to what has passed, so neither is the time of two months considered with the one menstruation if she menstruated once then became menopausal, nor is the time of one month considered with the two menstruations, but rather she must observe three independent months, so that combining the substitute and the substituted is not necessitated, for three months are a substitute for three menstruations. This is what the commentator al-Harawi mentioned, and by it he repelled the difficulty that the commentator raised. He said: 'I say...etc.' is an objection to what the author ruled for resumption, and that in two ways: First, the obligation of 'iddah follows the divorce and is required to be connected to it, but the assumption is that she was at the time of divorce one who menstruates until she menstruated once or twice, then she became menopausal afterwards, so it is not apparent that her 'iddah is by months from the time of divorce, so that three months become obligatory, because the menopause is delayed from the time of divorce, unlike what preceded, because seeing blood there after menopause judges that she was not menopausal from the time of divorce, but rather she was one who menstruates with extended purity, so the menstruations she saw before menopause contain time, so it must be counted from the 'iddah from the perspective that it is time. And regarding a woman observing 'iddah who was violated by a dubious contract with another 'iddah, and they overlap, then resumption with menstruation is required and what passed is invalidated, and here it is not apparent by the delayed menopause that she was menopausal from the time of divorce. Second, that if it appears that her 'iddah is by months from the time of divorce, then resumption is also meaningless, but rather the time of the one or two menstruations that preceded the menopause must be counted from the 'iddah, from the perspective that it is time, not from the perspective that it is menstruation. And it was answered: that the resumption is by not considering the previous time from the perspective of menstruation, not by not considering it absolutely, and this repels the second objection but does not repel the first, unless it is said that the delayed menopause is considered from the time of divorce to avoid combining the substitute and the substituted. He said: 'Regarding a woman observing 'iddah...etc.'; in the general term 'woman observing 'iddah' there is an indication of generalizing the ruling to 'iddah of divorce and 'iddah of death, and in the general term 'violation' there is an indication of generalizing the ruling to violation by the divorced husband and by someone else. He said in 'al-Durar Sharh al-Ghurar': The woman when two 'iddahs become obligatory upon her, either they are from two men or from one, in the second there is no doubt that the two 'iddahs overlap. See: Sharh Ibn Malik (folio 115a).
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school regarding the 'iddah of a woman who menstruates after divorce and then becomes menopausal. The matn states that such a woman must resume her 'iddah with three months from the time of menopause, ignoring the previous menstruation(s). The reasoning, as provided from al-Hidayah, is to avoid combining the substitute (three months) with the substituted (three menstrual cycles) — that is, she cannot count the time of the previous menstruation as part of the monthly 'iddah because that would mix the two alternatives. The commentator then raises two objections: first, that the 'iddah obligation should begin immediately after divorce, but here the menopause occurs later, so it is unclear why the 'iddah should be based on months from divorce; second, if it is based on months from divorce, then the time of the previous menstruation(s) should still count as time passing, not as menstrual cycles, so resumption is unnecessary. The answer is that resumption only disregards the previous time from the perspective of being a menstrual cycle, not from the perspective of time passing. However, the first objection remains unless we say the delayed menopause is considered from divorce to avoid combining the substitute and the substituted. This is a key principle: the 'iddah by months is an alternative (badal) for the 'iddah by menstrual cycles (mubdal), and they cannot be combined. The passage ends with a note about overlapping 'iddahs: if a woman has two 'iddahs (e.g., from divorce and from dubious intercourse), they overlap and each menstruation counts for both.

Key Terms
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before she can remarry.
إياس (iyas): Menopause; the cessation of menstruation usually after a certain age (typically 55 years in Hanafi fiqh).
بدل (badal): The substitute; here, the three months of iddah for a woman who does not menstruate.
مبدل (mubdal): The original; here, the three menstrual cycles of iddah for a woman who menstruates.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman is divorced while menstruating. She experiences one menstruation cycle after divorce, then reaches menopause (iyas). According to the Hanafi ruling in this text, how should she complete her iddah?
A) She counts that one menstruation and then completes two more months.
B) She restarts her iddah and observes three complete months from the time of menopause.
C) She observes iddah by menstrual cycles, waiting for two more menstruations.
D) She chooses whether to use months or menstrual cycles.

Key Takeaway
The iddah of a menopausal woman is by months, and if she menstruated after divorce before menopause, those menstruations are not counted towards the monthly iddah; she must start a fresh three-month period.

Overlapping 'Iddahs (Tadakhul): When Menstrual Periods Count for Two Waiting Periods
Explanation of the Hanafi ruling on overlapping waiting periods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tadakhul (overlapping 'iddahs) and apply it to scenarios where a woman in 'iddah is subjected to a second marriage-like union via shubha.

Core Idea
When two 'iddahs are due, they interfere and the same menstrual periods count for both.

The Hanafi school allows overlapping 'iddahs (tadakhul) when a woman in her waiting period is subjected to intercourse under a mistaken belief (shubha) that imposes a second 'iddah. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its conditions, and the reasoning behind counting each menstrual period for both 'iddahs simultaneously.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the occurrence of a second marriage-like union via shubha during an ongoing 'iddah. The ruling is that the two 'iddahs interfere (tadakhul) – they are served concurrently, not sequentially.
The ruling applies to both 'iddah of divorce and 'iddah of death.
2
Locate the dalil
The dalil is not a single text but the general principle that 'iddah is due for any marriage-like union (including shubha) and the logical implication that multiple 'iddahs can overlap when the underlying reason (istibra' al-rahim) can be fulfilled simultaneously.
The source cites al-Mabsut and al-'Inayah as authority.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The effective cause ('illah) is that the purpose of 'iddah – to ascertain purity of the womb and observe a waiting period – is achieved by the same menstrual cycle, so there is no need for separate sequences.
This reasoning is specific to the Hanafi school; other schools may require sequential 'iddahs.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Hanafi position allows tadakhul; other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) generally require each 'iddah to be observed separately. The passage affirms the Hanafi view as the relied-upon position.
The difference is based on whether the 'illah of 'iddah is single or multiple.

And in the first: if they are of two types, like a woman whose husband died and she is subjected to intercourse by shubha, or of one type like a divorced woman who marries during her 'iddah and the second husband has intercourse with her and they are separated, they interfere according to us, and whatever menstruation she sees is counted for both, and if the first 'iddah ends without the second, she must complete the second. If she had not seen any, then she only has three menstruations, and they substitute for six.' (3) And he said in al-Mabsut: 'If she married during the 'iddah of death and the second husband entered upon her and they were separated, she must complete the remainder of her 'iddah from the first – fully four months and ten days – and she must undergo three menstruations for the second, and what she menstruated after the separation is also counted from the 'iddah of death.' (4) [2] His saying: 'And this was understood' – meaning that the menstruation which is ruled upon as being counted from both is after intercourse by shubha.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on the ruling of overlapping 'iddahs (tadakhul). The core matn states: 'And regarding a woman in 'iddah who is subjected to intercourse by mistake (shubha) and another 'iddah is due, and they interfere.' The commentator, Imam Akmal al-Din al-Babarti, expands on this by explaining that the menstruation seen before menopause should be counted as time from the 'iddah. He then discusses an objection: if menopause occurs later, it shows she was already menopausal from the time of divorce, so restarting the 'iddah seems necessary, but that is refuted. The key point is that when two 'iddahs are due from one man (e.g., a divorced woman who is subjected to intercourse by shubha during her 'iddah), they interfere, meaning that each menstrual period counts for both 'iddahs simultaneously. The commentator distinguishes between cases where the two 'iddahs are of the same type (e.g., both from divorce) or different types (e.g., one from death, one from divorce). The Hanafi position is that interference (tadakhul) occurs in both situations, so the menstrual periods are counted for both. If the first 'iddah ends before the second, she must complete the remaining period for the second. The passage also clarifies that no 'iddah is due for outright adultery; the intercourse must involve some mistaken belief (shubha) for it to count. Examples are given: a woman married during her 'iddah of death and the second husband divorced her, she must complete the death 'iddah and also observe three menstrual periods for the second marriage; the menstruation she sees after separation counts for both. The commentary relies on sources like al-Mabsut, al-'Inayah, and al-Fath, establishing this as the relied-upon Hanafi ruling.

Key Terms
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death before she can remarry.
تداخل (tadakhul): Interference or overlapping of two 'iddahs such that a single menstrual period counts for both.
شبهة (shubha): A mistaken belief that intercourse is lawful, which still imposes an 'iddah obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A divorced woman in her 'iddah (due to three menstruations) is subjected to intercourse by shubha from another man. According to the Hanafi ruling on tadakhul, what happens?
A) She must restart both 'iddahs from the beginning.
B) The 'iddahs interfere, so the menstrual periods she sees count for both 'iddahs.
C) The second 'iddah is nullified because it is based on shubha.
D) She completes the first 'iddah with the original periods, then observes three new periods for the second.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on tadakhul allows a woman to count one set of menstrual periods for multiple overlapping 'iddahs, avoiding unnecessary repetition.

Talaq — Divorce

Interlocking of Iddahs (Tadakhul) in Cases of Shubha Intercourse
Explains Hanafi ruling on overlapping iddahs and contrasts with Shafi'i view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on interlocking (tadakhul) of iddahs when shubha intercourse occurs during an existing iddah, identify the Shafi'i counter-position, and explain the underlying rationale (ta'lil).

Core Idea
When two iddahs overlap, one complete iddah suffices because its purpose is to verify the womb's purity.

This lesson explores a nuanced scenario in Hanafi family law: what happens when a woman already observing an iddah (from divorce or death) engages in intercourse under a mistaken belief of lawfulness (shubha)? The Hanafi school holds that the two iddahs interlock and are fulfilled by one waiting period. We will examine the ruling, its evidence, and how it differs from the Shafi'i approach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario and Hanafi ruling
First, understand the situation: a woman in iddah (from divorce or death) engages in intercourse with shubha. According to Hanafi school, the two iddahs interlock (tadakhul) and are completed by one waiting period. Note that shubha is distinguished from zinā, which has no iddah.
The matn phrase 'wa hayd tarahu minhuma' is parsed: the menstruation seen after shubha counts for both iddahs.
2
Understand the rationale (ta'lil)
The Hanafi justification is that the purpose of iddah is to verify the purity of the womb (barā'at al-rahim). Since this purpose is achieved by a single complete iddah, requiring two separate iddahs would be redundant and cause unnecessary hardship.
The text states: 'المقصود من العدة هو تعرّف براءة الرحم' (the purpose of iddah is to determine the purity of the womb).
3
Compare with the Shafi'i position
Imām al-Shāfiʿī holds that iiddahs interlock only if the shubha intercourse is from the husband during her iddah from him. If from a stranger, they do not interlock. This is based on the idea that each iddah is a distinct obligation arising from a different cause.
The text says: 'أما عند الشافعي .. إن كان الوطء بالشبهة من الزوج، وهي في عدته، أما إن كان من آخر فلا'.
4
Apply to specific examples
Work through the examples from al-Ināyah and al-Mabsūt: if a woman in death iddah remarries with shubha, she completes the remainder of her death iddah, which also fulfills the shubha iddah. If a woman in divorce iddah has seen one menstruation before shubha, then after shubha she needs two more to complete the interlocked iddah (total three, not four).
These examples show how menstruations are counted across both iddahs.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference has real-world impact: Under Hanafi, a woman who experiences shubha intercourse during her iddah only needs to complete one waiting period. Under Shafi'i (if the shubha is from a stranger), she must complete two separate iddahs sequentially.
This is the 'thamarah al-khilaf' – a key concept in fiqh to appreciate why legal differences matter.

( وتنقضي " عدة الطلاق والموتِ وإن جهلت بهما) : أي بتطليق الزوج وموته، ومبدؤها عقيبهما) : أي عقيب الطلاق والموت.. [٤] قوله : إنّ هذا ؛ أي ما ذُكِرَ من تداخل العدتين مطلقاً، ، سواء كان الواطئ . المطلق أو غيره، ووجهه : إنّ المقصود من العدة هو تعرّف براءة الرحم، وهو يحصل بالعدة الواحدة، فيتداخلان مطلقاً. [۲] قوله : أما إن كان من آخر فلا ؛ فعنده لا تتداخل عدة الوفاة وعدة الوطء بشبهة، وكذا عدّة الطلاق وعدّة وطء غير المطلق بشبهة، وتتداخل عدة الطلاق وعدة وطء المطلق بشبهة ، وهذا إحدى الروايات عنه ، وفي رواية عنه : لا تداخل مطلقاً. [٣] قوله : وتنقضي ... الخ ؛ الحاصلُ أنَّ انقضاء العدة لا يتوقف على علمها بطلاقِ الزوج أو موته، بل تنقضي بانقضاء الأجل من وقتِ الطلاق أو الموت، وإن لم تعلم الزوجة به؛ لأنّ العدّة ،أجل، فلا يشترط العلم بمضيه، سواء اعترف بالطلاق أو أنكر ، فلو طلق ثم أنكر الطلاق وأقيمت البينة عليه، فقضى القاضي بالفرقة بعد زمان من الدعوى، فالعدّة من وقتِ الطلاق لا القضاء. كذا في «البزازية». [٤]قوله : عقيبهما ؛ أي متصلاً على الفور ؛ لأنّ وجوبها بسبب الموتِ والطلاق ، ويستثني منه الطلاق المبهم ؛ فإنّ العدّة فيه من وقتِ البيان لا من وقت الطلاق، فلو قال : إحداكما طالق ولم يبين ومات قبل البيان لَزِمَ كلاً من زوجتيه عدة الوفاة
And menstruation that she sees from both: 'Hayd' is the subject, 'tarahu' is its attribute, and 'minhuma' is its predicate, meaning menstruation that she sees after intercourse with shubha, and this is understood from the fact that 'wuti'at' is a past tense verb, and 'tarahu' is a future tense verb, and 'minhuma' means from the two iddahs. In the first case: if it is of two types, like a woman whose husband has died and she is subject to shubha intercourse, or of one type like a divorced woman who remarries during her iddah and the second husband has intercourse with her, then they are separated, the two iddahs interlock according to us, and what she sees of menstruation is counted for both, and if the first iddah ends without the second, then she must complete the second. And the restriction of intercourse with shubha indicates that there is no iddah in zinā. So if he has intercourse with her after the third divorce during the iddah without marriage, knowing its prohibition, no iddah is required for this intercourse because it is zinā. But if he has intercourse with her without marriage after he had divorced her with kināyah words, due to shubha that they were returning, according to what some held, or after the third divorce with or without marriage if he said: 'I thought she was permissible for me,' then iddah is required. Thus in al-Fath. [1] His saying: 'And menstruation that she sees from both'; Al-Ināyah said: 'Its form is: if the second intercourse occurs after the woman has seen one menstruation, then after the second intercourse, three menstruations are also required, and the two menstruations are a substitute for four menstruations: two for the first and two for the second, and the third is specifically for the second intercourse. If she had not seen anything, then only three menstruations are upon her, and they substitute for six.' And al-Mabsūt said: 'If she married during the iddah of death and the second husband entered her, then they were separated, then she must complete her iddah from the first fully four months and ten days, and she must observe three menstruations for the latter, and those she menstruated after separation are also counted from the iddah of death.' [2] His saying: 'And this has been understood' means that the menstruation that is ruled to be counted from both occurs after intercourse with shubha. Then if the first ends without the second, she must complete it. And the iddah of divorce and death expires even if she is unaware of them, and its beginning is immediately after them. And know that this is our madhhab. As for al-Shāfiʿī, according to him they interlock if the intercourse with shubha is from the husband while she is in his iddah. But if it is from someone else, then not. Then if the first ends without the second, she must complete it. Its form: The husband divorced her irrevocably or three times, then she menstruated once, then someone other than the husband has intercourse with her in shubha. Then she has two iddahs. The first menstruation is from the first iddah, and two menstruations after it are from both iddahs. So the first iddah ends, and a fourth menstruation is required to complete the second iddah. And the iddah of divorce and death expires even if she is unaware of them. That is, by the husband's divorce and death. And its beginning is immediately after them: i.e., immediately after divorce and death. [4] His saying: 'Indeed this' means what has been mentioned about the interlocking of the two iddahs absolutely, whether the one who had intercourse is the husband or someone else. Its rationale is that the purpose of the iddah is to determine the purity of the womb, and this is achieved by one iddah, so they interlock absolutely. [2] His saying: 'As for if it is from someone else, then not' means according to him, the iddah of death and the iddah of shubha intercourse do not interlock, and likewise the iddah of divorce and the iddah of intercourse by someone other than the husband with shubha do not interlock. But the iddah of divorce and the iddah of intercourse by the husband with shubha do interlock. This is one narration from him. Another narration from him: no interlocking at all. [3] His saying: 'And it expires... etc.' The summary is that the expiration of the iddah does not depend on her knowledge of the husband's divorce or death. Rather, it expires by the passage of the period from the time of divorce or death, even if the wife does not know, because the iddah is a period. So knowledge of its passage is not required, whether he admits the divorce or denies it. So if he divorces and then denies the divorce, and evidence is established against him, and the judge rules for separation after some time from the claim, then the iddah is from the time of divorce, not from the judgment. Thus in al-Bazzāziyyah. [4] His saying: 'Immediately after them' means connected immediately, because its obligation is due to death and divorce. An exception to this is ambiguous divorce (mubham), for the iddah in that case is from the time of clarification, not from the time of divorce. So if he says: 'One of you two is divorced' and does not specify, and he dies before clarification, then each of his wives must observe the iddah of death.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi ruling on overlapping iddahs (تداخل العدة) when a woman in iddah has intercourse with shubha (semblance of marriage). The author begins by parsing the matn phrase 'wa hayd tarahu minhuma' from the original text. He identifies that the menstruation seen after shubha intercourse is counted for both iddahs when they overlap. The ruling is that if a woman in iddah (either from death or divorce) is subject to a second valid (though irregular) union that is later separated, the two iddahs interlock and are fulfilled together. For example, a widow who is married in shubha during her death iddah, or a divorced woman who remarries during her divorce iddah – when separated, she only needs to complete one combined iddah, not two separate ones. The author then clarifies that there is no iddah for zinā (fornication). The key distinction is that shubha intercourse (where the woman thought it was lawful) triggers an iddah, while knowing zinā does not. The author quotes from al-Ināyah and al-Mabsūt to illustrate how the menstruations are counted across both iddahs. Next, he presents the Shāfiʿī view: according to Imām al-Shāfiʿī, the iddahs only interlock if the shubha intercourse is from the husband during her iddah from him; if it is from another man, they do not interlock. The Hanafī position is that interlocking is absolute, regardless of who the man is, because the purpose of iddah is to verify the purity of the womb, which is achieved by one complete iddah. The passage also discusses the start and end of iddah: it begins immediately after the divorce or death, even if the woman is unaware. An exception is ambiguous divorce (mubham), where the iddah begins only after the ambiguity is resolved. The author cites al-Bazzāziyyah to emphasize that if a husband denies divorce but it is proven, the iddah is counted from the time of divorce, not from the court ruling. Overall, this passage clarifies a nuanced scenario in family law and reinforces the Hanafī commitment to the underlying rationale (maqāṣid) in determining legal rulings.

Key Terms
عدة (ʿiddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband before she can remarry.
تداخل (tadākhul): Interlocking or overlapping of two iddahs such that they are fulfilled simultaneously by one set of menstruations.
وطء بشبهة (waṭʾ bi-shubhah): Intercourse that occurs under the semblance of legality, e.g., when the woman believed the marriage was valid or had a mistaken belief that she was lawful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a woman in the iddah of death (from her first husband) is subject to shubha intercourse from a second man and then separated, what is the ruling on the two iddahs?
A) They must be observed separately; she completes both iddahs fully.
B) They interlock; a single iddah suffices for both.
C) She only needs to observe the iddah of death, the shubha intercourse has no iddah.
D) It depends on whether the second man was her husband or not.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, two overlapping iddahs interlock absolutely, because the sole purpose of the iddah is to verify the purity of the womb, which a single period accomplishes.
