Overlapping 'Iddahs with Wati' Shubha
Explains scenario of overlapping 'iddahs and Hanafi vs Shafi'i view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on overlapping 'iddahs and compare it with the Shafi'i view, identifying the practical consequence of the difference.

Core Idea
Two 'iddahs overlap in Hanafi law because one waiting period suffices to ascertain purity of the womb.

This lesson examines the ruling of overlapping 'iddahs (tadakhul) when a woman in her 'iddah has intercourse under shubha. The Hanafi school permits complete overlap, while the Shafi'i school restricts it. Understanding this difference is crucial for correctly determining a woman's waiting period.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Determine what the Hanafi school holds regarding overlapping 'iddahs in the given scenario.
The text states the Hanafi view: absolute overlap, whether the second man is the husband or a stranger.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Identify the opposing school (Imam al-Shafi'i) and their position on the same scenario.
Shafi'i distinguishes: if the second act is by a non-husband, no overlap; if by the husband, there are two narrations.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the reasoning behind the Shafi'i position as implied in the text.
Each cause of 'iddah is independent and requires its own waiting period.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine how the Hanafi school rebuts the Shafi'i argument.
The purpose of 'iddah is to ascertain purity of the womb, achieved by one waiting period.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence of the difference between the two schools.
In Hanafi, one combined 'iddah suffices; in Shafi'i, separate 'iddahs may be required.

فإذا تمت الأولى دون الثانية يجب إتمامها) : صورته : طلقها الزوج بائناً، أو ثلاثاً فحاضت حيضة، فوطِئَها غيرُ الزوج بشبهة ، فعليها عدتان ، فالحيضةُ الأُولى من العدة الأولى، وحيضتان بعدها تكونان من العدتين، فتمت العدةُ الأُولى، فتجب حيضة رابعة ليتمم العدَّةَ الثانية. [۲] قوله : أما إن كان من آخر فلا ؛ فعنده لا تتداخل عدة الوفاة وعدة الوطء بشبهة، وكذا عدّة الطلاق وعدّة وطء غير المطلق بشبهة، وتتداخل عدة الطلاق وعدة وطء المطلق بشبهة ، وهذا إحدى الروايات عنه ، وفي رواية عنه : لا تداخل مطلقاً.
If the first (iddah) is completed without the second, it is obligatory to complete it. Its scenario: The husband divorces her with a ba'in divorce or three divorces, then she menstruates once, then a non-husband has intercourse with her by shubha. She must observe two 'iddahs. The first menstruation counts for the first 'iddah, and two menstruations after that count for both 'iddahs. So the first 'iddah is completed, and a fourth menstruation becomes obligatory to complete the second 'iddah. The reasoning is that the purpose of 'iddah is to ascertain the purity of the womb, and that is achieved by one 'iddah, so they overlap absolutely. [2] He said: 'As for if it is from someone else, then no' according to him (al-Shafi'i): the 'iddah of death and the 'iddah of intercourse by shubha do not overlap, nor does the 'iddah of divorce and the 'iddah of intercourse by a non-divorcer by shubha, but the 'iddah of divorce and the 'iddah of intercourse by the divorcer by shubha do overlap. This is one narration from him, and in another narration from him: no overlapping at all.

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of overlapping (tadakhul) 'iddahs when a woman in her 'iddah from a ba'in or triple divorce is had intercourse with shubha by another man. The Hanafi school holds that the two 'iddahs overlap entirely—the woman completes them together as one period. The commentator clarifies that this is based on the purpose of 'iddah: ensuring the womb is free from pregnancy, which is achieved by a single waiting period. The Shafi'i school, in contrast, distinguishes: if the second man is the husband himself, there is overlap; if a stranger, no overlap, requiring two separate 'iddahs. The text notes a second Shafi'i narration that denies overlap absolutely. The Hanafi position is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) in the madhhab.

Key Terms
عدة (ʿiddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death before remarrying.
وطء بشبهة (waṭʾ bi shubhah): Sexual intercourse under a mistaken belief that the union is lawful (e.g., due to a faulty marriage or mistaken identity).
تداخل (tadākhul): Overlapping or consolidation of two 'iddah periods into one.

The 'Iddah Period: Beginning and End Without Knowledge
Explains that 'iddah ends without woman's knowledge and begins immediately after cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rules for when 'iddah begins and ends in cases of divorce, death, ambiguous divorce, and past acknowledgment, and explain the rationale.

Core Idea
The 'iddah is a fixed term; it begins at the cause and ends automatically regardless of the woman's knowledge.

This lesson addresses two fundamental principles of 'iddah: its automatic expiration without the woman's knowledge, and its immediate commencement upon the cause. These rules are justified by the legal nature of 'iddah as a fixed term (ajal). We will also explore exceptions for ambiguous divorce and past acknowledgment.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule for the end of 'iddah
Identify the rule: 'iddah ends even if the woman is ignorant of the divorce or death. The passage states it ends with the expiry of the period from the time of the cause.
This applies whether the husband acknowledges the divorce or denies it; a court ruling does not reset the start.
2
Identify the rational proof (dalil aqli)
The reason is that 'iddah is an ajal (fixed term). Knowledge of its passing is not a condition for its expiry; time itself determines the end.
This is a rational proof based on the definition of 'iddah as a time-bound obligation.
3
State the general rule for the beginning of 'iddah
The 'iddah begins immediately after the cause (divorce or death), i.e., at the moment the cause occurs.
No gap is allowed; the obligation attaches instantaneously.
4
Identify the exception: ambiguous divorce (talaq mubham)
If the husband says 'one of you is divorced' without specifying, the 'iddah begins from the time of clarification, not from the utterance. If he dies before clarifying, both wives observe the 'iddah of death.
The divorce is not effective until the wife is identified, so the cause is incomplete until clarification.
5
Note the acknowledgment case and its rationale
If a husband acknowledges divorcing his wife in the past, the 'iddah is calculated from the time of acknowledgment, not the alleged earlier date. This is to prevent collusion (muwada'ah) between spouses to achieve legal benefits.
This protects the rights of other potential marriages (e.g., marrying a sister or a fourth wife).

Source Text

[٣] قوله : وتنقضي ... الخ ؛ الحاصلُ أنَّ انقضاء العدة لا يتوقف على علمهـا بطلاقِ الزوج أو موته، بل تنقضي بانقضاء الأجل من وقتِ الطلاق أو الموت، وإن لم تعلم الزوجة به؛ لأنّ العدّة أجل، فلا يشترط العلم بمضيه، سواء اعترف بالطلاق أو أنكر ، فلو طلق ثم أنكر الطلاق وأقيمت البينة عليه، فقضى القاضي بالفرقة بعد زمان من الدعوى، فالعدّة من وقتِ الطلاق لا القضاء. كذا في «البزازية». [٤]قوله : عقيبهما ؛ أي متصلاً على الفور ؛ لأنّ وجوبها بسبب الموتِ والطلاق ، ويستثني منه الطلاق المبهم ؛ فإنّ العدّة فيه من وقتِ البيان لا من وقت الطلاق، فلو قال : إحداكما طالق ولم يبين ومات قبل البيان لَزِمَ كلاً من زوجتيه عدة الوفاة، تستكمل فيها ثلاث حيض، وإن بين مراده بالمبهم تعتد من وقت البيان لا من وقتِ الطلاق. كذا في «البحر». ولو أقر بطلاقها منذ زمان ماض تكون العدّة فيه من زمان الإقرار، نفياً لتهمة المواضعة ؛ أي الموافقة على الطلاق، وانقضاء العدة ليصح إقرار المريض لها بالدين، أو ليتزوج أختها أو رابعاً سواها. كذا في «الفتح».
And the 'iddah of divorce and death ends even if she is ignorant of them. (Meaning: by the divorcing of the husband and his death.) And its beginning is immediately after them. (Meaning: immediately after divorce and death.) [3] He said: 'And it ends...' The summary is that the completion of the 'iddah does not depend on her knowledge of the husband's divorce or death; rather it ends with the expiry of the period from the time of divorce or death, even if the wife does not know it, because the 'iddah is a set term; knowledge of its passing is not required, whether he acknowledges the divorce or denies it. So if he divorces, then denies the divorce and evidence is established against him, and the judge rules for separation after some time from the claim, the 'iddah is from the time of divorce, not the judge's ruling. Thus in al-Bazzaziyyah. [4] He said: 'Immediately after them'; meaning connected immediately, because its obligation is due to death and divorce. An exception is the ambiguous divorce: for the 'iddah is from the time of clarification, not from the time of divorce. So if he says: 'One of you is divorced' and does not specify and dies before clarification, each of his wives must observe the 'iddah of death, completing three menstrual periods therein. And if he clarifies his intent regarding the ambiguous one, she observes 'iddah from the time of clarification, not from the time of divorce. Thus in al-Bahr. And if he acknowledges that he divorced her at a past time, the 'iddah is from the time of acknowledgment, to negate the suspicion of collusion (i.e., agreement on the divorce and end of 'iddah) so that the sick person's acknowledgment of a debt to her is valid, or so that he can marry her sister or a fourth wife. Thus in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage explains two key principles of 'iddah: First, the 'iddah period ends automatically with the passage of time from the cause (divorce or death), regardless of whether the woman knows about the divorce/death. The commentator justifies this by noting that 'iddah is a fixed term (ajal), and knowledge is not a condition for its expiry. This applies even if the husband denies the divorce; the court ruling does not reset the start. Second, the 'iddah begins immediately after the cause—the same moment divorce occurs or death happens. An exception is the ambiguous divorce (talaq mubham), where the husband says 'one of you is divorced' without specifying; the 'iddah for that divorce only begins when he clarifies which wife he intended. If he dies before clarification, both wives observe the 'iddah of death. Additionally, if a husband acknowledges divorcing his wife in the past, the 'iddah is calculated from the time of that acknowledgment, not the alleged earlier date, to prevent collusion. This protects the rights of other potential marriages (e.g., marrying a sister or a fourth wife).

Key Terms
طلاق مبهم (ṭalāq mubham): A divorce where the husband does not specify which wife is divorced, e.g., 'one of you is divorced'.
أجل (ajal): A fixed term or period; used here to describe the nature of 'iddah as a time-based obligation.
مواضعة (muwāḍaʿah): Collusion or agreement between spouses to falsely claim a past divorce to achieve legal objectives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The 'iddah is a fixed term that begins with the cause (divorce/death) and ends automatically with its passage, regardless of the woman's knowledge; special rules apply for ambiguous divorces and acknowledgments.

Commencement of 'Iddah in a Fasid Marriage
Clarifies two triggers for 'iddah in a fasid marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two triggers for 'iddah in a fasid marriage and explain why the husband's inner intention is insufficient.

Core Idea
In a fasid marriage, 'iddah begins only upon a judge's separation or expressed abandonment, not mere resolution.

In a valid (sahih) marriage, 'iddah begins immediately upon divorce. But what about a marriage that is defective (fasid)? The Hanafi school clarifies that 'iddah is still obligatory in such a marriage, but it begins upon one of two specific events. This lesson unpacks those triggers and why mere inner intention does not suffice.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish valid vs. invalid marriage for 'iddah
Note that in a valid marriage, 'iddah begins at the moment of divorce. In an invalid (fasid) marriage, it does not begin at the contract or divorce, but only upon actual separation.
2
Identify the first trigger: judge's separation (tafriq)
A judge's ruling that the marriage is invalid and orders separation starts the 'iddah.
3
Identify the second trigger: expressed abandonment (mutaraka)
The husband must express his decision to abandon intercourse, e.g., by saying 'I have left you.' Mere inner intention is not enough.
Evidence of the intention, such as verbal declaration, is required.
4
Understand why inner intention is insufficient
The 'iddah period has legal consequences, so it must be based on objective evidence, not subjective resolve. The husband must articulate his abandonment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي نكاح فاسد عقيب تفريقه ) ، أو عزمه ترك الوطء . [۱] قوله : وفي نكاح فاسد ؛ هو الذي فقد شرطاً من شروطه، والحاصل: إنّ العدة عقب الطلاق إنما هو في النكاح الصحيح، وأما في الفاسد، فعقيب أحد أمرين : إما تفريق القاضي، وهو أن يحكم بالفرقة بينهما. وإما المتاركة، وهو أن يعزم بقلبه ترك وطئها وليس المراد به مجرد العزم الباطني، بل لا بد من دليل عليه، وهو الإخبار . عنه ، كأن يقول : فارقتك، أو تركتك ، أو خليت سبيلك ، ومنه الطلاق. كذا في «البحر»، وغيره.
And in an invalid marriage, (it begins) after his separation, or his resolution to abandon intercourse. [1] He said: 'And in an invalid marriage': it is the one that lacks a condition from its conditions. The summary: The 'iddah after divorce applies only to a valid marriage. As for an invalid one, (it begins) after one of two things: either separation by the judge, which is that he rules for separation between them; or abandonment (mutaraka), which is that he resolves in his heart to abandon intercourse with her, but what is meant is not just internal resolution; rather there must be evidence for it, i.e., informing about it, such as saying 'I have separated from you' or 'I have left you' or 'I have let you go', and divorce is included. Thus in al-Bahr and others.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage clarifies the start of 'iddah in an invalid (fasid) marriage. A fasid marriage is one that lacks some condition (shart) of validity, so it is not sahih. In contrast to a valid marriage where 'iddah begins immediately upon divorce, in a fasid marriage the 'iddah begins upon one of two events: (1) a judge's ruling of separation (tafriq), or (2) the husband's actual abandonment (mutaraka), which requires more than a mere inner intention—he must express it, for example by saying 'I have left you' or 'I have set you free'. Divorce is also a form of this. The commentator emphasizes that a mere inner resolve without evidence is insufficient to start the 'iddah.

Key Terms
نكاح فاسد (nikāḥ fāsid): A marriage contract that lacks a condition of validity (like witness requirement) and is therefore invalid, but unlike batil, it may produce some effects like 'iddah.
تفريق (tafrīq): Separation ordered by a judge due to the invalidity of the marriage.
متاركة (mutārakah): Abandonment; the husband's decision to cease marital relations, expressed verbally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a fasid (invalid) marriage, when does the 'iddah begin?
A) At the time of the contract
B) Upon separation by a judge or expressed abandonment
C) Only upon the judge's ruling
D) Only upon the husband's inner intention to separate

Key Takeaway
In a fasid marriage, 'iddah begins only with an objective act: a judge's separation or the husband's verbal abandonment.

Woman's Claim of Completed 'Iddah: Conditions for Acceptance
Procedural ruling on accepting woman's iddah claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a woman's claim that her 'iddah has ended is accepted and apply the rule to scenarios with different time frames.

Core Idea
A woman's claim that her 'iddah has ended is accepted with an oath only if the elapsed time makes completion possible.

In Hanafi fiqh, a woman who claims her 'iddah has ended (e.g., to remarry) may face a dispute from her former husband. The school gives credence to her statement, but subject to two critical conditions: she must swear an oath, and the time elapsed must be sufficient for the 'iddah to have ended. This lesson explores the rule and the minimum durations that determine plausibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and the dispute
The woman says her 'iddah has ended; the husband disputes it. The question is whose word is accepted.
2
Determine if time permits completion
Check whether the elapsed time since the divorce is at least the minimum possible 'iddah period. For 'iddah by months, this is 3 months (free) or 1.5 months (slave). For 'iddah by menstruation, minimum 60 days (free) or 40 days (slave).
These minima assume shortest possible menstrual cycles.
3
Apply the oath requirement
If time permits, the woman must swear an oath that her 'iddah has ended. Her statement is then accepted.
If she refuses to swear, her claim is rejected.
4
Handle insufficient time
If the elapsed time is shorter than the minimum possible 'iddah, her claim is rejected outright without an oath, because it contradicts apparent reality.
E.g., claiming completion within one month when minimum is 60 days.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قالت : انقضت عدتى حُلْفَت) [٢]قوله : حلفت ؛ التحليف، يعني لو قالت المرأة: تمت عدتي، وكذا لو من نكحت زوجاً غيره، وقال الزوج : لم تنقض عدتك فالقول قولها مع اليمين، وهذا إذا كانت المدة تحتمل انقضائها، ففي العدّة بالشهور ثلاثة أشهر أو نصفه لأمة. وفي العدة بالحيض أقلها لحرّة ستون يوماً، ولأمة أربعون، وإن لم تكن المدة تحتمله كما إذا أخرت بانقضاء عدتها في شهر واحد لم يعتبر قولها ؛ لأن اليمين إنما يصدّق في قولها إذا لم يخالف الظاهر. كذا في «النهر » ، و«البحر»).
And if she says, 'My 'iddah has ended', she is made to take an oath. [2] He said: 'She is made to take an oath' means that if the woman says, 'My 'iddah is complete', and similarly if she marries another husband and the (first) husband says, 'Your 'iddah has not ended', then her statement is accepted with an oath. This is when the period permits its completion. So for 'iddah by months, it is three months or half that for a slave woman. For 'iddah by menstruation, the minimum for a free woman is sixty days, and for a slave woman forty days. And if the period does not permit it, as if she claims the end of her 'iddah within one month, her statement is not accepted, because the oath is only accepted in her statement if it does not contradict the apparent. Thus in al-Nahr and al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage addresses a procedural rule: when a woman claims her 'iddah has ended (e.g., to remarry), the husband may dispute it. The Hanafi school gives credence to the woman's statement, but only if she can plausibly have completed the 'iddah within the elapsed time. She must also swear an oath. The commentator specifies minimum durations: for 'iddah by months, a free woman must wait three months; for a slave woman, one and a half months. For 'iddah by menstruation (for a free woman divorced in a manner requiring three menstruations), the minimum possible is about 60 days (if one menstruation is 10 days and the interval is short); for a slave woman, 40 days. If she claims completion in a period too short to be possible (e.g., one month), her claim is rejected outright because it contradicts apparent reality. This protects the certainty of the waiting period.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamīn): Oath; the woman swears that her 'iddah has ended.
العدة بالشهور (al-ʿiddah bi al-shuhūr): The 'iddah calculated in months (for women who do not menstruate or are too young/old).
العدة بالحيض (al-ʿiddah bi al-ḥayḍ): The 'iddah calculated by menstrual cycles (generally three cycles).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is a woman's claim that her 'iddah has ended NOT accepted?
A) She refuses to take an oath
B) The time elapsed is too short for the 'iddah to have ended
C) The husband denies it
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
A woman's claim of completed 'iddah is accepted with an oath only if the elapsed time meets or exceeds the minimum possible duration for her type of 'iddah.

Iddah After Invalid Marriage and Woman's Claim of Completion
Explains when iddah begins after invalid marriage and woman's claim of completion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when the iddah begins after an invalid marriage in the Hanafi school and apply the rule regarding the woman's claim of completion of iddah.

Core Idea
In an invalid marriage, iddah begins from separation or declaration, not from divorce; the woman's word is taken with oath if time permits.

This lesson examines the rules of iddah after an invalid marriage as explained in the commentary on Al-Wiqaya. The passage clarifies that the iddah does not commence from the divorce pronouncement but from the actual separation or the husband's declared intention to abandon intercourse. It also addresses how a woman's claim that her iddah has ended is treated: her statement is accepted with an oath if the elapsed time could possibly encompass the iddah duration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the marriage type and its effect on iddah
Note that the passage distinguishes between valid and invalid (fasid) marriage. In a valid marriage, iddah follows divorce; in an invalid marriage, it follows separation or declaration, not divorce.
2
Name the two triggers for iddah in invalid marriage
Iddah begins after one of two events: the judge's decree of separation (tafriq) or the husband's declaration of abandoning intercourse (muatarakah). The inner resolve alone is insufficient; an explicit statement is required.
3
Explain the condition for the husband's intention to be effective
The husband's intention to leave must be evidenced by an announcement, such as 'I have separated from you' or similar words. Divorce is included in this category.
4
Determine when the woman's claim of completion is accepted
If the woman says her iddah has ended and the husband disputes, her statement is accepted with an oath, provided the elapsed time could possibly allow completion of the iddah. If the time is impossibly short, her claim is rejected.
Minimum durations: free woman: 60 days (3 menstruations) or 3 months; slave woman: 40 days or 1.5 months.
5
Apply the time constraints
If the woman claims completion in one month, it is impossible for three menstruations or three months, so her claim is not accepted. If the time is sufficient, her word is taken with oath.
The oath is to negate the possibility of collusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي نكاح فاسد عقيب تفريقه، أو عزمه ترك الوطء. ولو قالت : انقضت عدتي حلفت [1] (1) (وفي نكاح فاسد عقيب تفريقه ) ، أو عزمه ترك الوطء . ولو قالت : انقضت عدتى حُلْفَت) تستكمل فيها ثلاث حيض، وإن بين مراده بالمبهم تعتد من وقت البيان لا من وقتِ (۲) الطلاق. كذا في «البحر». ولو أقر بطلاقها منذ زمان ماض تكون العدّة فيه من زمان الإقرار، نفياً لتهمة المواضعة ؛ أي الموافقة على الطلاق، وانقضاء العدة ليصح إقرار المريض لها بالدين، أو ليتزوج أختها أو رابعاً سواها. كذا في «الفتح» (۳). وجوب أمرين : [۱] قوله : وفي نكاح فاسد ؛ هو الذي فقد شرطاً من شروطه، والحاصل: إنّ العدة عقب الطلاق إنما هو في النكاح الصحيح، وأما في الفاسد، فعقيب أحد أمرين : إما تفريق القاضي، وهو أن يحكم بالفرقة بينهما. وإما المتاركة، وهو أن يعزم بقلبه ترك وطئها وليس المراد به مجرد العزم الباطني، بل لا بد من دليل عليه، وهو الإخبار . عنه ، كأن يقول : فارقتك، أو تركتك ، أو خليت سبيلك ، ومنه الطلاق. كذا في «البحر»، وغيره. (٤) [۲]قوله : حلفت ؛ التحليف، يعني لو قالت المرأة: تمت عدتي، وكذا لو من نكحت زوجاً غيره، وقال الزوج : لم تنقض عدتك فالقول قولها مع اليمين، وهذا إذا كانت المدة تحتمل انقضائها، ففي العدّة بالشهور ثلاثة أشهر أو نصفه لأمة. وفي العدة بالحيض أقلها لحرّة ستون يوماً، ولأمة أربعون، وإن لم تكن المدة تحتمله كما إذا أخرت بانقضاء عدتها في شهر واحد لم يعتبر قولها ؛ لأن اليمين إنما (0) (1) يصدّق في قولها إذا لم يخالف الظاهر. كذا في «النهر » ، و«البحر»).
And in an invalid marriage, after separation or his resolve to abandon intercourse. And if she says: 'My waiting period has ended,' she is made to swear an oath. She completes three menstruations therein. If he clarifies the ambiguous [pronouncement], she begins the waiting period from the time of clarification, not from the time of divorce. Thus in 'al-Bahr'. And if he acknowledges divorcing her since a past time, the waiting period is from the time of acknowledgment, to negate suspicion of collusion—i.e., agreement on the divorce and completion of the waiting period, to validate the sick person's acknowledgment of debt to her, or to marry her sister or a fourth besides her. Thus in 'al-Fath'. [It is] obligatory from two matters: [1] His statement: 'And in an invalid marriage'—it is one that lacks a condition from its conditions. The conclusion: the waiting period after divorce is only in a valid marriage. As for the invalid, it is after one of two matters: either the judge's separation—that he rules for separation between them—or al-mutaraka—that he resolves in his heart to abandon intercourse. What is meant by it is not mere inner resolve; rather, evidence is necessary for it, namely the announcement of it, like his saying: 'I have separated from you,' or 'I have left you,' or 'I released you'; divorce is included in this. Thus in 'al-Bahr' and others. [2] His statement: 'She is made to swear'—the swearing of an oath. That is, if the woman says: 'My waiting period has ended,' and likewise if she marries another husband and the husband says: 'Your waiting period has not ended,' then her statement is accepted with an oath. This is when the time frame allows for its conclusion. For the waiting period by months, it is three months or half for a slave woman. For the waiting period by menstruation, the minimum for a free woman is sixty days, and for a slave woman forty days. If the time frame does not allow it—as if she claims the completion of her waiting period in one month—her statement is not considered because the oath is only believed in her statement if it does not contradict the apparent. Thus in 'al-Nahr' and 'al-Bahr'.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn followed by its commentary. It addresses two issues related to the waiting period (iddah) after an invalid marriage. First: For an invalid marriage (nikah fasid), the iddah does not begin at the time of divorce; rather, it begins either from the judge's decree of separation (tafriq) or from the husband's declared intention to abandon intercourse (muatarakah). The commentary clarifies that the 'intention' must be evidenced by an explicit statement such as 'I have left you.' Second: If the woman claims that her iddah has ended, and the husband denies it, the woman's statement is accepted with an oath, provided the time elapsed could possibly encompass the iddah. The commentary provides the minimum durations: for iddah by months, three months for a free woman and half that for a slave woman; for iddah by menstruation, minimum sixty days for a free woman and forty days for a slave woman. If the time claimed is impossibly short, her claim is rejected. This follows the Hanafi position that the woman's word is taken when it does not contradict external reality.

Key Terms
نكاح فاسد (nikāḥ fāsid): A marriage that lacks one of its essential conditions, such as witnesses or proper consent.
عدة (ʿiddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarrying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The time frame could possibly allow it.
B) She provides two witnesses.
C) The husband agrees.
D) She has not remarried.

Key Takeaway
Iddah after an invalid marriage begins from the actual separation or declared intention, and the woman's claim of completion is accepted with oath if the time could include the minimum waiting period.

Full Mahr and Separate Iddah for Marriage in Bā'in Iddah
Ruling on full mahr and separate iddah when marrying one's own iddah woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a man marries his bā'in iddah wife and divorces before consummation, and explain the legal principle (first intercourse effect) that underpins it.

Core Idea
Marrying one's own bā'in iddah wife triggers full mahr and a new iddah because the first intercourse's effect persists.

This lesson examines a specific scenario from the Hanafi fiqh of divorce and marriage: a man remarries his wife during her irrevocable (bā'in) waiting period and then divorces her before consummation. The text rules that he owes a full dower (mahr tāmm) and she must observe a separate iddah. We will unpack the conditions for this ruling, the legal basis, and an intra-madhhab difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is marrying one's own woman during her bā'in iddah (not revocable or three-fold) and then divorcing her before consummation. The ruling: full mahr wajib on husband, separate iddah on wife.
This is not a general rule for all marriages in iddah; it is specific to one's own iddah woman.
2
Distinguish the type of iddah
The iddah must be from a bā'in divorce other than the three-fold. If revocable, the second contract is a revocation; if three-fold, remarriage is prohibited.
Bā'in here means the first or second irrevocable divorce, not the third.
3
Note the condition of valid marriage
The marriage must be valid (sahih). If it is fasid, no mahr and no new iddah; she completes her original iddah.
Validity of the second marriage is crucial.
4
Understand the legal principle: effect of first intercourse
The first intercourse's effect persists in the iddah period, so the woman is still considered in the husband's grasp (qabda). This makes the second marriage effective as if he had consummated it juristically.
This is one of the ten masāʾil ʿashr where the first entry counts as entry in the second.
5
Recognize the intra-Hanafi difference
The text attributes this ruling to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf; Imam Muhammad might hold a different view, possibly requiring actual consummation for such effects.
The muftā bihi is the stated position from the matn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو نكح معتدته من بائن وطلقها قبل الوطء فعليه مهر تام، وعدة مستقلة [2] [0] [1] قوله : وكذبها الزوج ؛ ولو ادّعى الزوج انقضاء عدتها، وغرضه حل نكاح أختها وأربع سواها، وكذبته في مدة تحتمله لم تسقط نفقتها، وله نكاح أختها حملاً بخبريهما بقدر الإمكان، فلو ولدت لأكثر من نصف حول، يثبت نسبه، ولم يفسد نكاح أختها في الأصح فترثه لو مات دون المعتدة. كذا في «المحيط» وغيره، والتفصيل في «الدر المختار وحواشيه. [٢] قوله : ولو نكح ؛ أي نكاحاً صحيحاً ، ولو تزوجها في العدة نكاحاً فاسداً فلا مهر، ولا استئناف عدة، بل عليها إتمام العدّة الأولى اتفاقاً ؛ لأنه لا يتمكن من الوطء في النكاح الفاسد، فلا يجعل واطئاً حكماً ؛ لعدم إمكان الحقيقة ؛ ولذا لا تجب عدة ولا مهر بالخلوة الفاسدة، والنكاح الأوّل يعم الصحيح والفاسد، فلو نكح بامرأة فاسداً ودخل بها ففرق بينهما، ثم تزوّجها صحيحاً في العدة، فالحكم هو الذي ذكره في المتن. كذا في «البحر». [۳] قوله : معتدته ؛ الضمير إلى الناكح ؛ أي التي طلقها سابقاً فاعتدت، ولو نكح معتدة الغير ووطئها تتداخل العدّتان كما مرّ. [٤] قوله : من بائن ؛ متعلق بقوله : «معتدته»، والمراد به غير ثلاث، وذلك لأنه لو كانت معتدته من رجعي فالعقد الثاني رجعة، ولو من ثلاث لم تحل له حتى تنكح زوجاً غيره. [0]قوله : قبل الوطء ؛ سواء كان حقيقة أو حكماً، يعني قبل الوطء والخلوة. (۱) لأنها مقبوضة في يده بالوطء الأول لبقاء أثره، وهو العدة وهذه إحدى المسائل العشر المبنية على أن الدخول في النكاح الأول دخول في الثاني. ينظر : (الدر المختار»(٢ : ٦١٣).
And if he marries his woman who is in the waiting period of an irrevocable divorce and divorces her before intercourse, then he must pay a full dower and she has a separate waiting period. [2] [0] [1] His statement: 'And if the husband denies her'—and if the husband claims the completion of her waiting period, aiming for the permissibility of marrying her sister or a fourth besides her, and she denies him in a time frame that allows it, her maintenance does not fall, and he may marry her sister, taking both statements as much as possible. If she gives birth after more than half a year, his lineage is established, and his marriage to her sister is not invalidated in the most correct view, so she inherits from him if he dies before the iddah woman. Thus in 'al-Muhit' and others, with details in 'al-Durr al-Mukhtar' and its glosses. [2] His statement: 'And if he marries'—meaning a valid marriage. If he marries her during the waiting period with an invalid marriage, then no dower and no renewal of waiting period; rather, she must complete the first waiting period by agreement, because he is not able to consummate in the invalid marriage, so he is not considered to have intercourse juristically due to impossibility of actuality. Hence, no waiting period or dower is due with an invalid seclusion. The first marriage includes both valid and invalid. If he married a woman invalidly and had intercourse and then separated, then married her validly during the waiting period, the ruling is that which the text mentions. Thus in 'al-Bahr'. [3] His statement: 'his woman who is in the waiting period'—the pronoun refers to the husband, i.e., the one he divorced earlier and she is in iddah. If he marries another's iddah woman and has intercourse, the two iddahs overlap as previously. [4] His statement: 'of an irrevocable divorce'—related to his statement 'his iddah woman', meaning other than three-fold irrevocable divorce, because if her iddah is from a revocable divorce, the second contract is a revocation, and if from a three-fold divorce, she is not lawful to him until she marries another husband. [0] His statement: 'before intercourse'—whether actual or juristic, meaning before intercourse and seclusion. (1) Because she is in his grasp through the first intercourse due to the persistence of its effect, which is the iddah, and this is one of the ten issues based on the principle that entering into the first marriage constitutes entering into the second. See: al-Durr al-Mukhtar, 2:613.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the matn: If a man marries his former wife during her bā'in (irrevocable) iddah and then divorces her before consummation, he owes her a full dower (mahr tamm) and she must observe a separate iddah from this second marriage. The commentary elaborates on several sub-points. First, it clarifies that the 'iddah woman' here refers to the man's own previously divorced wife in the bā'in iddah (not including revocable or three-fold irrevocable). Second, it specifies that the marriage in iddah must be valid; if it is invalid, no mahr is due and the woman completes her original iddah. Third, it discusses the scenario where the husband claims the iddah has ended, leading to various rulings on maintenance and marriage to the sister. The legal basis is the principle that the first intercourse's effect persists, so the woman is still considered in the man's grasp, thus the second marriage triggers full mahr and a new iddah. This is one of the ten famous issues (al-masāʾil al-ʿashr) where cohabitation in the first marriage is considered cohabitation in the second. The commentary attributes this ruling to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, implying possible difference from Muhammad.

Key Terms
معتدة من بائن (muʿtaddah min bāʾin): A woman who is observing the waiting period after an irrevocable divorce (talaq bā'in) that is not a three-fold divorce.
مهر تام (mahr tāmm): The full dower specified in the marriage contract or the equivalent dower, paid by the husband upon divorce before consummation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling if a man marries his former wife during her bā'in iddah and then divorces her before consummation?
A) No mahr is due, and she completes her original iddah.
B) He must pay half the mahr, and she observes one iddah.
C) He must pay full mahr, and she observes a separate iddah.
D) The marriage is void, and no legal consequences apply.

Key Takeaway
A marriage during bā'in iddah followed by pre-consummation divorce obligates full mahr and a separate iddah due to the persistent effect of the first intercourse.

Marrying One's Idda: Constructive Consummation in Hanafi Fiqh
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mahr and idda

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding full mahr and a second idda when a man marries his idda from a bain divorce and divorces before consummation, and contrast it with Muhammad al-Shaybani's position.

Core Idea
The ongoing effect of the first intercourse (the idda) constructs consummation in the second marriage, triggering full mahr and a new idda.

This passage presents a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute. When a man divorces his wife revocably (bain), then marries her again during her idda, and divorces her before consummating the new marriage, does he owe full or half mahr? Does she observe a new idda? The answer hinges on whether the first intercourse's effect is considered present in the second marriage. Let's unpack the reasoning and practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Identify the facts: a man marries his own idda from a bain divorce, then divorces her before any actual intercourse in the new marriage.
The idda is from a bain divorce (not triple), so the couple can remarry.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's ruling
They rule: full mahr (as after consummation) and an independent idda for the second divorce.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
3
Understand the talil of the two shaykhs
The reasoning: the effect of the first intercourse (the idda) is still present, so it is as if intercourse occurred in the second marriage, making the divorce after consummation.
The idda is considered an athar (trace) of the first intercourse.
4
Present Muhammad al-Shaybani's counter-argument
Muhammad argues: since no actual intercourse happened in the second marriage, only half mahr is due, and no idda for the second divorce; she simply completes her first idda.
He does not consider the idda as a sufficient trace to construct consummation.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical difference is full vs. half mahr and a second idda vs. none. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
The matn and later commentaries favour the two shaykhs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو نكح معتدته من بائن وطلقها قبل الوطء فعليه مهر تام، وعدة مستقلة. هذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. فإن أثر الوطء في النكاح الأول باق، وهو العدة، فصار كأن الوطء حاصل في هذا النكاح. وعند محمد ليس عليه إلا نصف المهر، وإتمام العدة الأولى فقط، ولا عدة للطلاق الثاني؛ لأن الزوج طلقها قبل الوطء فيه.
And if he marries his woman who is in her 'idda from a revocable (bā'in) divorce, and divorces her before intercourse, then a full mahr is due upon him, and an independent 'idda. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Indeed, the effect of intercourse in the first marriage remains, which is the 'idda, so it is as if intercourse occurred in this marriage. And according to Muhammad, only half the mahr is due upon him, and only completing the first 'idda, and no 'idda for the second divorce; because the husband divorced her before intercourse in it.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a man who divorces his wife revocably (bā'in), then marries her again while she is still in her 'idda from that divorce, and then divorces her before consummating the new marriage. The ruling in the matn (according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) is that a full mahr is obligatory and she must observe a new, independent 'idda for the second divorce. The reasoning (talil) is that the effect of the first intercourse—namely, the 'idda—is still ongoing, so the first intercourse is considered as if it occurred in the second marriage, making the subsequent divorce after consummation. Thus, full mahr and a new 'idda are due. Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that only half the mahr is required, and she only needs to complete her first 'idda; no new 'idda for the second divorce because it occurred before intercourse. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is the amount of mahr and the necessity of a second 'idda. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, as indicated by the matn and the fact that the commentary defends it.

Key Terms
معتدة (mu'taddah): A woman observing her 'idda (waiting period) after divorce or death.
بائن (bā'in): A revocable (irreversible) divorce where the husband cannot take the wife back without a new marriage contract and her consent.
مهر تام (mahr tāmm): Full dower, usually the amount stipulated in the marriage contract, due when consummation (actual or constructive) occurs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the second marriage contract itself makes the mahr obligatory.
B) Because the first intercourse is considered as having occurred in the second marriage due to the ongoing effect of the idda.
C) Because the husband was deceptive and must pay full mahr as a penalty.
D) Because the second marriage was invalid, so full mahr is a compensation.

Key Takeaway
The ongoing effect of the first intercourse (the idda) makes the second marriage as if consummated, requiring full mahr and a second idda according to the relied-upon Hanafi view.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on 'Iddah and Mahr After Marriage During First 'Iddah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mahr and 'iddah after second marriage during first 'iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions regarding mahr and 'iddah when a woman in 'iddah remarries and is divorced before consummation, and evaluate the reasoning of each imam.

Core Idea
The first 'iddah persists through a second marriage; its effect renders the second husband liable for mahr and prevents a new 'iddah.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: a woman observing 'iddah from a first marriage enters a second marriage but is divorced before consummation. Do the rules of the second marriage apply normally, or does the lingering effect of the first marriage override them? The Hanafi imams offer three distinct answers, each relying on different principles of effect (athar) and obligation (wujub). Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to apply 'illah and istihsan in family law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and legal questions
Note the facts: a woman in 'iddah from a first marriage contracts a second marriage, then the second husband divorces her before consummation. Two issues arise: how much mahr is due from the second husband, and what 'iddah (if any) must she observe after the second divorce?
The first 'iddah is still ongoing from the first husband.
2
State the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
They rule: full mahr is due from the second husband, she completes her first 'iddah (the one from the first husband), and no new 'iddah arises from the second divorce. Their reasoning: the effect of the first intercourse (i.e., the 'iddah) persists, so the second husband is treated as if he had intercourse in the second marriage, triggering full mahr and no new 'iddah.
This is an analogy (qiyas) that treats the 'iddah as a continuing effect.
3
State the view of Muhammad al-Shaybani
He rules: half mahr is due, she completes her first 'iddah, and no new 'iddah. His reasoning: since there was no actual intercourse in the second marriage, the divorce before consummation halves the mahr (general rule), and no 'iddah arises without intercourse. The first 'iddah remains because it was already obligatory.
Muhammad disagrees with Abu Hanifa on mahr but agrees on the first 'iddah.
4
State the view of Zufar
He rules: half mahr, but no 'iddah at all—not even completing the first 'iddah. His reasoning: the second marriage nullifies the obligation of the first 'iddah, and a dropped obligation cannot return. Since the second marriage ended without consummation, she is free to remarry immediately.
Zufar agrees with Muhammad on mahr but differs on the 'iddah.
5
Evaluate the tarjih and the refutation of Zufar
The commentary refutes Zufar: the first 'iddah was not dropped; it merely became latent during the second marriage. Once the second marriage dissolves, the original obligation resumes. Additionally, canceling 'iddah entirely defeats its purpose of preventing lineage confusion. The mufta bihi (operative position) is that the first 'iddah must be completed (majority view), and half mahr is due (Muhammad's view, as more consistent with general principles).
The text from 'al-Fath' and 'al-Bahr' rejects Zufar's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أثر الوطء في النكاح الأَوَّلِ باق، وهو العدة، فصار كان الوطء حاصل في هذا النكاح (۳) وعند محمد يجب عليه نصف المهر، وإتمام العدَّةِ الأُولَى فقط، ولا عدَّةَ للطلاق الثاني ؛ لأنَّ الزَّوجَ طلقها قبل الوطء فيه : (۱) قوله : فإنّ ... الخ ؛ حاصل وجه قولهما: أن أثر النكاح الأول باق بعد وهو العدة، فصار كأنها مقبوضة في يده بالوطء الأوّل، فيجعل كأنه وطء في هذا النكاح وطلّق بعده ، فيجب كمال المهر، وعليها العدة. لا يقال : الطلاق بعد الدخول يملك به الرجعة، ولا رجعة هاهنا لأنا نقول : لا يلزم من إقامته مقام الوطء في العقد الثاني في حق المهر والعدة أن يقوم مقامه في حق الرجعة أيضاً. كذا في «منح الغفار». [٢] قوله : حاصل في هذا النكاح ؛ هذا من إحدى المسائل التي يكون الدخولُ فيها في النكاح الأول دخولا في الثاني. والثانية : تزوج معتدته وهو مريض وطلقها قبل الدخول، فيكون فاراً. والثالثة : فرّق بينهما بعدم الكفاءة بعد الدخول، فنكحها في العدة، وفرّق بينهما قبل الدخول. والرابعة : تزوج صغيرة أو أمة ودخل بها، ثمّ أبانها ثم تزوجها في العدة فبلغت أو أعتقت فاختارت نفسها قبل الدخول. والخامسة : تزوّج صغيرة أو أمة فاختارت نفسها بالبلوغ أو العتق بعد الدخول، ثم تزوجها في العدة، ثمّ طلّقها قبل الدخول. والسادسة : تزوج معتدته فارتدت قبل الدخول. كذا في «البحر»، وغيره. [٣]قوله : وعند محمد ... الخ ؛ حاصل مذهبه أنه يجب على الزوج نصف المهر ؛ لكونه طلقها بعد النكاح الثاني قبل الدخول ، والطلاق قبل الدخول منصف للمهر، ولا عدة عليها بهذا الطلاق؛ إذ لا عدّة في الطلاق قبل الوطء، بل يجب عليها إتمام العدة الأولى. (۱) (البحر الرائق (٤ : ١٦١ - ١٦٢). (1) وعند زُفَر لا عدة عليها أصلاً ؛ لأن العدةَ الأُولَى سقطت بالتزوج، ولم يجب بالنكاح الثاني لدليل محمد. [1]قوله : وعند زفر ... الخ ؛ قد وافق زفر محمدا في وجوب نصف المهر عليه فحسب، وفي عدم وجوب العدّة للطلاقِ الثاني، لدليل ذكر في توجيه قول محمد ، وخالفه في وجوب إتمام العدة مستنداً بأن العدة الأولى قد سقط وجوبها عليها بالتزوّج الثاني، والساقط لا يعود فلا يجب عليها شيء من التربص، بل يجوز لها أن تنكح كما طلق زوجها الأول. ورد بأن وجوب العدّة الأولى بالطلاق الأوّل، فيجب إكماله، إلا أنه لم يظهر حال التزوج الثاني، فإذا طلقها ثانياً قبل الدخول صار النكاح الثاني معدوماً، فيجب عليها إكمال ما وجب أولاً مع أن في عدم إيجاب العدّة عليها مطلقاً إبطال لما هو المقصود من شرعيّة العدة، وهو عدم اختلاط الأنساب واشتباهها. كذا في «الفتح»، وغيره.
Thus the effect of intercourse in the first marriage remains, which is the 'iddah, so it becomes as if intercourse occurred in this marriage. (3) According to Muhammad, half the mahr is obligatory upon him, and only the completion of the first 'iddah, and no 'iddah for the second divorce, because the husband divorced her before intercourse in it. (1) His statement: 'Thus...' etc.; the summary of their (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's) view: the effect of the first marriage remains, which is the 'iddah, so she becomes as if she is in his possession by the first intercourse, so it is considered as if he had intercourse in this marriage and divorced after it, so full mahr is obligatory, and 'iddah is upon her. It cannot be said: divorce after consummation gives the right of return, and there is no return here, because we say: it does not follow that placing it in the position of intercourse in the second contract regarding mahr and 'iddah means it also takes its place regarding return. Thus in 'Minh al-Ghaffar'. [2] His statement: 'occurred in this marriage'; this is one of the cases in which consummation in the first marriage counts as consummation in the second. The second: he married his woman in 'iddah while ill and divorced her before consummation, so he is a fugitive. The third: separation due to lack of compatibility after consummation, so he married her during 'iddah, and separation occurred before consummation. The fourth: he married a minor or a slave-girl and consummated, then irrevocably divorced her, then married her during 'iddah and she reached puberty or was freed and chose herself before consummation. The fifth: he married a minor or a slave-girl and she chose herself by puberty or freedom after consummation, then he married her during 'iddah, then divorced her before consummation. The sixth: he married his woman in 'iddah and she apostatized before consummation. Thus in 'al-Bahr' and others. [3] His statement: 'According to Muhammad...' etc.; the summary of his doctrine is that half the mahr is obligatory upon the husband because he divorced her after the second marriage before consummation, and divorce before consummation halves the mahr, and no 'iddah is upon her due to this divorce, since there is no 'iddah in divorce before intercourse; rather she must complete the first 'iddah. (1) (al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 4:161-162). (1) And according to Zufar, no 'iddah is due upon her at all, because the first 'iddah fell by the marriage, and it is not obligatory by the second marriage according to the proof of Muhammad. [1] His statement: 'According to Zufar...' etc.; Zufar agreed with Muhammad regarding the obligation of half the mahr only, and regarding the non-obligation of 'iddah for the second divorce, due to the proof mentioned in the explanation of Muhammad's statement, but he disagreed regarding the obligation of completing the 'iddah, based on the fact that the obligation of the first 'iddah fell by the second marriage, and what has fallen does not return, so nothing of waiting is obligatory upon her; rather it is permissible for her to marry as soon as her first husband divorced her. It was refuted by saying that the obligation of the first 'iddah was by the first divorce, so it must be completed, but it was not apparent during the second marriage; when he divorced her a second time before consummation, the second marriage becomes void, so she must complete what was first obligatory, and in addition, not obligating any 'iddah at all nullifies the purpose of legislating 'iddah, which is to avoid mixing of lineages and confusion. Thus in 'al-Fath' and others.
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a woman who, while in the 'iddah (waiting period) from a first marriage, enters a second marriage and is then divorced by the second husband before consummation. The discussion revolves around the effect of the first marriage's 'iddah on the obligations of the second marriage. The majority view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf maintains that the effect of the first intercourse persists through the 'iddah, so the second husband is treated as if he had intercourse (even though he did not) in the second marriage, thereby making full mahr obligatory and requiring completion of the first 'iddah only—no new 'iddah arises from the second divorce. They offer an analogy: just as consummation in the first marriage makes the woman 'in his possession', the ongoing 'iddah carries that effect into the second contract; however, this analogy is limited to mahr and 'iddah and does not extend to the right of return (رجعة). Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees, ruling that only half the mahr is due (because the divorce occurred before consummation in the second marriage), and she completes only the first 'iddah; no 'iddah arises from the second divorce. Zufar goes further, arguing that the first 'iddah was nullified by the second marriage itself, and since obligation cannot return once dropped, she is free to marry immediately upon the second divorce, with only half mahr due. The commentary refutes Zufar by emphasizing that the first 'iddah was necessitated by the first divorce and remains intact albeit latent during the second marriage; once the second marriage is dissolved, the original 'iddah resumes. Moreover, abandoning the 'iddah entirely undermines its essential purpose of preventing lineage confusion. The passage also lists six similar cases where the first marriage's consummation is treated as consummation in the second marriage for legal purposes. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the first 'iddah must be completed, and only half mahr is due per Muhammad's view (or full mahr per Abu Hanifa—the text does not explicitly tarjih, but the response to Zufar aligns with the majority on 'iddah, leaving the mahr issue unresolved).

Key Terms
العدة (al-ʿiddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband before remarrying.
المهر (al-mahr): The bridal gift (dower) that the husband must pay to the wife upon marriage; its amount may be specified or default.
الطلاق (al-ṭalāq): Divorce, the dissolution of marriage by the husband's pronouncement.
الدخول (al-dukhūl): Actual sexual intercourse or valid seclusion (khalwa sahiha) that establishes the full effects of marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, what mahr is due when a woman in 'iddah remarries and is divorced before consummation in the second marriage?
A) Full mahr, because the first marriage's effect carries over.
B) Half mahr, because divorce before consummation halves the mahr.
C) No mahr, because the marriage was invalid.
D) Full mahr, because the second husband must pay full mahr regardless.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a second marriage during the first 'iddah does not cancel the original 'iddah; it remains latent, and its completion is wajib, though the mahr from the second marriage is halved if not consummated.

No 'Iddah for Dhimmīyah Divorced by Dhimmī
Ruling with objection and answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on 'iddah for a dhimmīyah divorced by a dhimmī and explain the rationale based on the nature of 'iddah as a right.

Core Idea
A dhimmīyah divorced by a dhimmī has no 'iddah because Islamic 'iddah is a right of the Muslim husband.

This passage from 'Umadat al-Ri'ayah clarifies a key Hanafi ruling: non-Muslim women are not bound by Islamic 'iddah when both spouses are non-Muslim. The commentator addresses a potential objection regarding a Muslim marrying such a woman immediately after divorce. By working through this text, you will understand the legal basis for exempting non-Muslims from 'iddah obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the opening statement: 'No 'iddah upon a dhimmīyah divorced by a dhimmī.' State the ruling in your own words.
The ruling is clear and unconditional for this scenario.
2
Understand the objection and answer
The commentator raises an objection: how can a Muslim marry such a woman immediately while he believes 'iddah is obligatory? Note the answer: Muslims believe 'iddah is obligatory only for themselves, not for disbelievers.
This reveals the underlying principle: 'iddah is a right of the Muslim husband, not a universal obligation.
3
Note the qualification when the husband is Muslim
The passage states that if the woman is a Kitābiyyah under a Muslim husband (i.e., he divorces her or dies), then 'iddah is obligatory by consensus.
This confirms that 'iddah is tied to the husband's religious affiliation.
4
Recognise the underlying principle
Synthesise: 'iddah in Islamic law is a right of the Muslim husband for preserving lineage. For non-Muslims, we do not impose Islamic 'iddah; their own laws govern.
The ruling is consistent with the Hanafi principle that non-Muslims are not addressed by Islamic commands in matters exclusive to Muslim marriage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا عدة على ذمية طلقها ذمي [۱] قوله : ولا عدة ؛ أي لا تجب العدّة على ذميّة طُلقت من ذمي، فيحل تزوجها كما طلقت، مسلماً كان المتزوج أو ذمّياً. كذا في «الفتح»، وغيره. وأورد عليه : بأنه كيف يحلّ تزوّج مسلم بها على الفور، وهو يعتقد وجوب العدة. وأجيب عنه : بأنه إنّما يعتقد وجوبها لنفسه ولسائر المسلمين، ولا يعتقد وجوبها لكافر. (۳) قوله : على ذميّة طلّقها ذمي ؛ الجملة صفة لذميّة، وذكر الذمية ؛ لعدم تصوّر أن يطلق الذمي مسلمة ؛ لعدم حلّ النكاح بين الكافر والمسلمة، وذكر كون المطلق ذمياً احترازي، فإنه لو كانت الذمية من أهل الكتاب تحت مسلم فطلقها ، أو مات عنها تجب العدة. (۱) ولو تزوجها مسلم أو ذمي في فور طلاقها جاز، وهذا إذا كانت لا تجب في معتقدهم بخلاف ما إذا طلقها المسلم أو مات عنها، فإن عليها العدة بالاتفاق ؛ لأنها حقه في معتقده
And there is no 'iddah upon a dhimmīyah divorced by a dhimmī. [1] His statement: 'no 'iddah'; i.e., the 'iddah is not obligatory upon a dhimmīyah divorced by a dhimmī, so it is permissible to marry her immediately after her divorce, whether the new husband is Muslim or dhimmī. Thus in 'al-Fath' and others. An objection was raised against it: how can it be permissible for a Muslim to marry her immediately, while he believes in the obligation of 'iddah? The answer: he only believes it is obligatory for himself and for all Muslims, but he does not believe it is obligatory for a disbeliever. (3) His statement: 'upon a dhimmīyah divorced by a dhimmī'; the phrase is a descriptive clause for dhimmīyah, and he mentioned dhimmīyah because it is inconceivable that a dhimmī would divorce a Muslim woman, since marriage between a non-Muslim man and a Muslim woman is not lawful. And mentioning that the divorcer is a dhimmī is precautionary; if the dhimmīyah were a Scriptuary under a Muslim and he divorced her, or he died on her, 'iddah would be obligatory. (1) And if a Muslim or dhimmī marries her immediately after her divorce, it is permissible, and this is when it [the 'iddah] is not obligatory in their belief, unlike if a Muslim divorced her or died on her, for then she must observe 'iddah by agreement, because it is his right in his belief.
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage states a clear ruling: a non-Muslim woman (dhimmīyah) who is divorced by her non-Muslim husband (dhimmī) is not required to observe any 'iddah (waiting period). Consequently, she may marry immediately after the divorce, and a Muslim man may wed her without delay. The commentator addresses a potential objection: a Muslim contracting marriage with such a woman believes 'iddah is obligatory for Muslims, but he does not believe it is obligatory for non-Muslims in their own marriages, so there is no contradiction. The ruling is qualified: it applies only when the divorcer is a dhimmī. If the woman were a Scriptuary (Kitābiyyah) married to a Muslim and then divorced or widowed by him, she would be subject to 'iddah by consensus. The rationale is that 'iddah in Islamic law is a right of the Muslim husband (or deceased) for lineage preservation, but for non-Muslims, their own religious laws govern, and since we do not impose Islamic 'iddah on them, the woman is free to remarry. This ruling is found in the authoritative works of the Hanafi school (al-Fath, etc.).

Key Terms
ذمية (dhimmīyah): A non-Muslim woman living under the protection (dhimma) of an Islamic state, typically a Christian or Jew.
عدة ('iddah): Waiting period after divorce or death; generally obligatory for Muslim women to ensure no pregnancy from the previous marriage.
ذمي (dhimmī): A non-Muslim male subject of the Islamic state who enjoys protection and is allowed to practice his religion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is 'iddah obligatory upon a dhimmīyah according to the Hanafi ruling in this passage?
A) Always, after any divorce or death of her husband.
B) Only if she is divorced by a Muslim husband or he dies.
C) Only if she divorces her husband.
D) Never, because she is a non-Muslim.

Key Takeaway
A dhimmīyah divorced by a dhimmī has no 'iddah; the ruling stems from the principle that Islamic 'iddah is a right for the Muslim husband only.
